A Galliard''s Tale

By Sam Inabinet

I can see their fire past the trees and brambles, and
now I'm beginning to catch their voices, mumbling their
way around songs that they can't even remember all the
words to anymore. This is even worse than I feared.

Sliding quietly through a thicket, I get my first good
look at them. Fewer than a dozen, all in Lupus or human-
form, except for an ancient metis ina blanket by the fire.
They're gathered on a flat promontory which overlooks
the Pass and affords a broad view of the wastes which
form the frozen northernmost stretch of the Plain of the
Apocalypse. At the peak of the promontory, facing the
wastes, sits a great hulking brute, perhaps the only
Ahroun in the lot. His jaw has sunk to his breast, and his
snoring would rattle Gaia's bones. All the rest are lolling
about the fire like a pack of moon calves.

When the wind turns, I get a snoutful of fumes from
that cauldron they've all been dipping their jacks into.
Luna only knows what they could've found around here
to brew it from, or how long they've been knocking it
back.

"Gawds above, but I am bored!" howls a particularly
obese specimen.

"You say that every damn night, Gorbuc!" his mates
snap back at him.

"Well, I'm bored every damn night! How long have
we been here, anyway?"

"Longer than your sotted knob can reckon," wheezes
the old one, "an' we'll be here ‘til them Wyrmspawn out
there makes their move! Now either say something
useful or shaddap!"

"Leastways if one of us was a Moon Dancer these here
night watches wouldn't take so long to pass," sighs a
round-faced pup.

With eyes thinas razor slits, the old one hisses through
clenched gums, "I. Am. A. Moon. Dancer."

"So let's have us a tale, then, eh?" I say, tossing my
words into the center of the fire. Give ‘em a chance, I'm
thinking; this may not be as bad as it looks. But the pack
breaks into some of the most pitiful wails you ever did
hear.

"No! Not again!"

"Spare us!"

"For the love of Gaia!"

"He's takin' a deep breath — somebody stop ‘im
quick!"

But no one actually makes a move, and the elder
gathers his breath and starts.

"Well, seems there's this ol' Silent Strider, see, an'
he's been stridin' all day long, an' he stops at this here
farmhouse fer to put ‘is paws up fer the night..."

"Is this the one where the farmer's daughter turns out
to be a faerie changeling?" I toss in.

Legends of the Garou


" ‘Course it's that fookin' one!" bellows the one they
called Gorbuc. "‘S the only fookin' one he fookin' knows
already!"

"Hold on, Gorbuc. Shut your hole for a minute,"
mutters a husky she-wolf, her gaze swaying back and
forth across the pack. "Who was it said that, now?"

For a moment the pack is quiet, looking stupidly from
one to another.

"‘S the bloody fomors done snuck up on us at last!"
cries a lanky hatchet-faced ape, staggering to his feet.
"To arms! Battle stations!" He clears the fire and a few
of his fellows in an impressively awkward bound and
dashes into the thicket a few yards from me, where, with
the aid of some clinging undergrowth and a low-hanging
branch, he executes a triple somersault and lands in a
gasping heap.

"Now why'd ye go and get poor Colin riled like that,
Flameback?"

"Yeah, prob'ly just some banshee having us on..."

"If the Wyrmspawn was comin', Burle'd see ‘em from
miles off."

"Who're you foolin'? Burle'd sleep through the bloody
Apocalypse!"

"He sleeps with one eye open, you know..

"Sure. And I'ma fookin' Celestine, ye didn't know?"

"If you don't believe it, go over there and see for
yourselves."

"Nah, that's all right."

"We'll take your word for it."

Awen help me, but I may be too late to do any good
here at all.

"Anyway, the War Council's so far away that this pass
ain't doin' anybody any good. Us least of all! What is it
we're to be doin' here? What would the bloody fomora
get by takin' this pass?"

"An easy victory, for one," | say with my own mouth
as | step into the firelight, "and a clear uncontested
march straight up the Council's backside, for another."

Teeth are bared, feral fur stands on end and homid
hands clench into fists. Every bloodshot eye on the
promontory turns upon me, all save for Burle, who keeps
sending those deep rumbling snores rolling out across
the broad empty wastes.

"You wanta story?" I say, my voice soft, but my posture
challenging. "Here's one: Seems there was this Silent
Strider, an anruth of course, and he had been charged by
the War Council with patrolling all the border watches,
making sure they were on their guard, that they were
alert to any enemy action and ready to take care of it
when it came."

"We don't need no stories!" growls Gorbuc. "Who
bleedin' asked ye, anyhow?"

"You did — with your hearts, if not your tongues.
Now, in every camp he passed through, this anruth saw

Garou strong and bright and sharp, breathing the full-
ness of their Rage and keeping it honed against the stone
of frequent conflict. But as he moved out toward the
Great Wastes, where even the Corrupter's minions are
loathe to tred, he found morale low and supplies rare.
Athros wise in war had fallen to bickering over old tribal
quarrels, and unbloodied cliaths with too much time
between their teeth would devise any excuse to be at
their fosterns' throats. Worst of all, though, were the
outposts.

"Here, even the pride of the tribes themselves had
fallen by the wayside, with lone isolated packs tending to
hated duties or wandering aimlessly around some half-
remembered mission. Hardened warriors grew soft from
want of battle, and keen eyes rolled in slumber. Purpose
was lost, and sloth and resentment crowded in to fill its
place. The Litany was sung no more, and old ways were
forgotten.

"One lookout post, far beyond the reach of the War
Council and the company of the Changing Breed, the
anruth found held by a pack composed entirely of kinain
of one of the truest and most noble tribes of Garou who
have ever walked in the ways of Gaia. Here, he thought,
stagnation can surely have taken no root, for those of
this lineage are nothing if not Lovers of the Ways.

"But the Corrupter had learned much of subtlety
during this long war, of how holding back from conflict
could serve Its purposes better than open assault. Using
Its absence as Its weapon, It fanned the dark flames of
discontent in the hearts of this noble pack."

For the most part, my audience has hardly stirred,
regarding me through dull and bleary eyes. The face of
the old metis is folded into a twisted toothless scowl, and
the one they called Flameback glares at me from beneath
bristling red brows. What little attention of theirs | do
hold, | hold only by virtue of the fact that mine is the first
new tale to reach their ears since they came to take this
post. But that is not enough. More than their attention
must be drawn from them tonight, and more than words
must rouse them.

"What the Silent Strider saw filled his heart with
sorrow. The pack had degenerated into a foul mockery of
the tribe that had nurtured them. The claw which
cannot find flesh to rend will curve too long, tearing at
the very paw which wields it. So was this Strider taught
in his youth, and so did he find it to be at this cold and
lonely outpost. Left without an enemy into which it
could sink its fangs, Rage had turned in upon itself,
devouring its tail and the heart in which it lived. Wis-
dom had fled at the heels of tradition, and tongues which
had once spun threads of silver were set to shoveling
slag."

As I speak, I pace slowly around the fire. The younger
ones turn their heads to follow me, but Gorbuc and a few
of the alpha-types resentfully pretend to ignore me.

\

"The Harano held them in its grip, and Gaia's greatest
gift to their tribe they squandered like spoiled pups!" —
and here I kick their pot of foul grog into the firepit.

The flames flare up, tempers but a half-step behind.
Steam billows into homid eyes, and sparks arc into lupus
fur. My audience erupts into a stamping, slapping, roll-
ing mass of ire and oaths, but the first part of my task is
accomplished: the fabric of their minds has unraveled
enough for me to grasp the dangling threads and leap
with them into the safety of a nearby tree.

"Aargh! Wha' the —!"

"Grab ‘im, quick!"

"Where's he got to?!"

"Tl braid the bastard's guts for a girdle!"

They have forgotten what it means to have a Galliard
among them. Their re-education begins now. Spreading
my words to surround them, I continue: "‘You'll have to
catch me first!' taunted the anruth. The pack, unused to
any foe but each other, fell upon their fostern in their
anger."

"Watch where the hell you're flailin', Gorbuc!"

"Shaddap! Outta my way, then!"

"Be still, all of you!" snapped Flameback. "He's play-
ing us for fools here!"

"I knew it! All them fookin' Striders is Wyrm fetid!"

"Yes, they had figured out the old Silent Strider's
secret." Drawing the threads of their fears together, I
weave them into a dream beast, vile and monstrous with
many heads bearing the faces of the pack itself. If they
must be blind to any foe but each other, then this is the
best way to reach them with the truth.

But I must open their eyes so that they should see the
true Enemy. To this end, I embellish the beast with the
most unsavory aspects of fomori and Black Spiral Dancer,
red eyes rolling with madness and jaws dripping with
green froth. Let them think that I am the corrupted one,
revealing my true form. Let them see me as the Enemy,
for if | can turn their Rage from one another to me, then
perhaps I can turn it across the wastes to its rightful
targets.

"Rumor had proved true, and the Enemy had strode
into their camp in guise of Garou!" So saying, I
unleash the dream beast in their midst, pulling the tale
and the told together as one.

"Red Rage ignited the pack, swelling them into the
half-form and whetting their appetite for slaughter. The
hoary metis croaked out the Anthem of War and was
joined by the pack. The challenge was returned by their
own voices, twisted into the Song of Mockery.

"They spread themselves out, the better to surround
the thing, but with its many heads it met their gaze from
every direction. ‘You think the Striders to be Urrah, and
so this one shall be!' it spat. ‘Forsake the Ways and join
those who would vanquish you! It's not such a great step
to take from where you now stand...'


" ‘Begone, Corrupter!' barked the old one. ‘Tempt us
not with your empty threats and shallow promises!'

"You would bite me, you fangless charach-spawn!?
Then join your miserable parents in Erebus, mule!" With
a roar the beast leapt at the old one, shearing hairless
skull from twisted spine and bathing its mock-mule face
in arterial spray.

"What followed might have been described as a flower
of Berserk ravage — were there any to see it from above
— as the Urrah-beast bloomed petals of Garou flesh. A
cliath drunk with self-loathing slashed at her own image,
which seemed a reflection in a rippling pool of gore.
Some sought their rival's visage; Flameback's jaws locked
upon the throat of the Urrah-Gorbuc, and vice-versa.
Full fell the blows, as of mallet to drumhead, deep the
growl of anger and high the scream of agony.

"But the Corrupter's tactic had backfired, you see. To
mock with their own faces those who already hate them-
selves is but to fan the flames of rage. The pack that held
this outpost had been only a step away from slicing each
other to ribbons; now they were provided with the very
targets which their last shreds of loyalty and purpose had
denied them.

"For that is both the strength and the weakness of
creatures of rage such as ourselves, difference between —
" and there ends the tale of my dream beast, for a wiry
hand closes about my snout, and long sinewy limbs pin
my arms to my sides.

"] got ‘im!" My captor's bark is shrill in my ear as | am
flung back into the circle of firelight. "I got the sneaky
bastard, I did!"

The pack crowds close around me, all Crinos now, but
whole and unbloodied by any fight. Eye and fang flashes
red in the fire.

"Colin! Now who'd've thunk that!"

"Good work, laddie! 1 knew ye had it in ye!"

"Nearly did us all up a treat, but for you!"

"Wyrmsign."

"Do ‘im! Do the fookin' Urrah git!"

Colin stands over me, his narrow chest heaving.
Flameback steps up to growl in his ear, "G'wan, Colin.
Throat ‘im. And don't you be feeling that you need to
stop at that, neither. Serve him right, it would, for
messing about with our minds the way he's done!"

Colin's head lowers, locking my gaze with his own,
and a gapped and snaggled grin slowly works its way
across his face. "Ain't never throated anyone afore..."

"Wyrmsign!"

"Gawds above! Lemme do it!" Gorbuc bellows, stomp-
ing forward. "Bloody New-Moon ain't got the Rage in
‘im to scratch a bloody flea!"

Flameback surges to intercept him. "Back off! This
here is Colin's catch!" But before the two archrivals can
stare each other down, Colin has me.

Now I can die here, and that would be fine if this pass
could be held indefinitely. But not even the Silver Pack
could prevail for long against the hordes that must
eventually sweep through here; time is the most that can
be hoped for, time to alert the War Council, time for the
tribes to regroup. My death now would serve none but
the Wyrm.

A wily old Stargazer once taught me a trick for just
such an occasion, a trick of stretching and twisting and
shifting, and this trick I now try. But Lam nota wily old
Stargazer; the best that | can manage is to shift Colin's
jaws from my throat to my —

"| SEZ WYRMSIGN, YA BLOODY GREAT MOON
CALVES!"

Colin's jaws are suddenly slack around the chunk he
was about to tear from my shoulder; all ears jump stiffly
to attention; all eyes turn to the one they call Burle.

He stands upon his promontory, glowering at our
antics, with one beefy arm pointing in the direction of
the wastes. From one end to the other, the horizon has
begun to blur with great clouds of dust. We are all on our
feet faster than can be told.

"This is it!" snaps the metis, a sharp new strength in
his voice. "Make yerselves ready!"

Gorbuc, his broad face suddenly inches from mine,
growls, "If I'm to go down here, then this one falls beside
me!" And, between the grog-fumes on his breath and the
rage coming off him in palpable waves, I nearly agree.

"No he don't, Gorbuc," Flameback says evenly. "Some-
one must carry word of this attack to the War Council,
or we die for nothing."

"But we decided..."

"Yes we did," snaps the metis impatiently, "but I'm too
old, Colin's too addled, and no one else can be spared.
And besides, Gorbuc," he adds with a sly wink, "who
else'll know to sing your praises at the victory moot?"

"Now how in the bloody hell is he goin' to do that if
he ain't even here for it?!"

"How about this?" I say to Gorbuc. "You kill the
most;" I nod at Flameback, "she kills the best;" and I
point to Burle, "and he's the last one standing before the
pass is overrun!"

For a moment they all look from one to another, then
at me; slowly laughter spreads among them, dark laugh-
ter echoing the creak of the gallows and carrionbird
calls.

"Right enough, then," says Gorbuc, "but every Wyrm-
fetid throat I rip out is gonna have yer stupid face above
it!"

"All and good, Gorbuc," I say, giving him what I have
been told is my most infuriating grin.

"So is the bloody pleasantries all bloody done with,
then?" Burle grumbles, shouldering the largest broadaxe
I have ever seen. He snorts derisively and sonorously.
"Right, pups! Let's get tore in at these slugs!"

6 Werewolf Storytellers Handbook


And then all throats stretch as one to the Anthem of
War, and with one will the fostern bound down the cliff
paths to their battle stations, howling and barking, gnash-
ing their teeth and sharpening their claws on stones. Out
across the wastes, the dust cloud has risen a bit higher into
the sky, and through it can be seen the hordes of fomori and
Black Spiral Dancers like the foam on cresting waves, and
beyond them rear the faceless necks of Thunderwyrms,
Nexus Crawlers, unnamed tentacled monstrosities and
things too disgusting to mention.

Something claps against my chest. Colin is offering me
a sloshing stoppered aleskin. "We got no more use fer this,
an' it'll keep yer paws from goin' numb when ye cross the
glacier. Don't worry," he adds with that jagged Ragabash
grin, "it don't taste as bad as it smell

"Aye, and lad," howls the old metis from down the path,
"when ye sing our Hymns of Praise, watch them purpley
passages ye seem to like so much. Makes ‘em hungry if they

ain't eaten, an' puts ‘em to sleep if they has."
*eH

And so Anukhet Whiptongue, the renowned Harbinger
of the Silent Striders, ended his tale with the same abrupt-
ness and lack of ceremony that began it. He arose and made
to leave the circle of the moot, for he was a busy Garou, and
there were yet many places he had to be on this night. Over
one sinewy shoulder, he howled his farewell to those
assembled.

"Fianna audiences are the greatest audiences in the
Realm! Thank you, good night!"

Chronicle Construction

This section builds on Chapter Three: Storytelling in
Werewolf: The Apocalypse. All of the guidelines on the
next few pages are suggestions. As always, use what you like
and disregard the rest. The only measure of successful
storytelling is whether you and your players have a good
time.

A Gwinkle in the Mind's Eye

Communicate communicate
Via satellite and solid state
Never never hesitate
Communicate communicate

~ Pete Townshend, "Communication"

Chapter One: Storytelling

[EE ET nema 7 -

Every great story comes from someone's thoughts. Some-
thing — a song, a novel, a conversation — turns on the
creative engine. Before running a story or starting a
chronicle, the best thing a Storyteller can do is just spend
some time thinking, creatively imagining scenes, designing
mannerisms for NPCs or wondering what the players will
do when they face their next dilemma. Keeping a small pad
of paper around throughout this thought process helps,

because great ideas can hit at the strangest times, then

vanish in the gulfs between sessions.

Always have a set of notes somewhere if you intend to
run a chronicle, even if they are illegible scratchings on a
dozen mangled index cards, Notes hardly have to be com-
plete, but they should contain enough information to
ensure that ifa stressful month passes between sessions, you
don't have to face the embarrassment of asking your players
what was the last name of one of your major NPCs.

Il


Before talking to your players or even announcing the
chronicle, get a sheet of paper and something to write with.
Sit and think about Werewolf. Just relax and write down
anything that comes to mind. Words, sentence fragments
— draw doodles if you want, just cover the page with stuff.
Then, sit back and look at what you've created, what kind
of patterns you've made. Usually, you'll get a good feel for
the kind of game you'll feel comfortable running.

If you can't come up with anything, then flip through
this book and Werewolf: The Apocalypse, watch a movie,
take a walk, or whatever, and come back to the chronicle
later. Don't stress out about Storytelling before you start
Sometimes ideas like to hide, but they most always come
out

Once you have something, see if you can find a predomi-
nant sense of mood or theme in what you've written. Most
of the time you should be able to narrow the scope of the
chronicle down to a few options for mood or theme. You
want to keep your ideas fairly simple. Werewolf is an
interactive game, and players can make the simplest
storylines incredibly complicated. Few Storytellers ever
complete a highly complex chronicle to their satisfaction.

If you can't keep your creative juices in check and want
to write the Great Garou Epic, break up your ideas and

make up two chronicles instead of one overly complicated

on e which of the two you like more and run with it. If

you can't decide, discuss both with your potential players

and see which one they'd prefer to play. Save the extra
chronicle for a later date.

Now's the time to go get help. After all, you can't play
Werewolf by yourself (unless you're a mage with a high
Sphere rating in Mind).

Example of Chronicle Creation: The
Wastes of Thule

Art has purchased Werewolf: The Apocalypse and he
loves it. He wants to be a Storyteller and start a chronicle.
He starts thinking about what type of chronicle he'd run.

He looks through the rulebook, and a line describing
Antarctica as Thule strikes him. He likes the thought of
Garou struggling across a continent of ice. He also loves the
stories of explorers like Shackleton who survived adversity
while trying to find the South Pole.

Art likes the Warren Zevon song, "Werewolves of Lon-
don," although he could care less about the movie. For some
reason, the howling chorus stays with him when he thinks
about the game. He decides that he has to do something set
in the British Isles.

Art starts making a list of elements that he wants for the
chronicle:

- London

- Big Ben

- Apocalypse

- British scientists

- Mount Erebus

-Cold

- Dragons

- St. George

- Music

- Glaciers

- Chemical weapons

- Powerful corporations

- Secret societies

- Animal experimentation
- What's underneath the ice in Antarctica?
- Insanity

- Bones

- Dark forests

- Underground British rock
- Nightclubs

- The moon

Some of it seems to work, some of it doesn't. Art thinks
that maybe if a corporation and some scientists in Britain
were trying to do something Wyrmish in Antarctica, he'd
have a fun chronicle. He also definitely needs a British
nightclub as a mainstay hangout somewhere. He goes to
talk to his unsuspecting friends, who are now potential
players. He decides to tell them to think up characters who
are in the United Kingdom.

ee Ae

Players: The Other
Storytellers

Werewolf is an interactive game. Players collaborate
with the Storyteller to make a good session, a good story or
a good chronicle. No matter how hard a Storyteller works,
the ultimate success or failure of a story (in terms of
entertainment) lies in the hands of the players and their
characters. A Storyteller opens up options for players,
letting them exert control over elements of the game. A
good player takes the reins, does her part, and then hands
them right back to the Storyteller. Some players cause
problems, but that's another matter.

When you start creating a chronicle, make sure you
consider the tastes of your players. No matter how much
you like them, don't invite someone to a game whom you
know will be difficult for the rest of the group. You can
create a solo chronicle for someone who doesn't fit the
troupe. Everyone doesn't enjoy the same type of stories, and
players are better off not participating in a game that they
won't like. Personality conflicts are one of the leading
killers of chronicles. Also, difficult doesn't necessarily
mean disruptive or annoying. Some players may only be
available on a certain night or once a month at times that
conflict with everyone else's schedule. Many players have
enough logistical problems just making it to sessions on
time. Plan away as many real world conflicts as possible
before they ever have a chance to start. Consider your own
availability to run games as well. Don't settle on a time
that's barely convenient.

Have your players make up characters and take notes on
them. Talk to your players and try to find out what their
characters want. What are their character's goals and
dreams? What are the character's weaknesses and faults?
Check the Traits that the player chooses. Make sure that
you and the player agree on the limits of abilities. For
example, if a player makes up a character who drives a
motorcycle, they need a dot in Drive. A character who
drives a motorcycle well will need two dots. While anyone
can try to drive a motorcycle, without a dot in Drive they
have to roll their Dexterity asa Dice Pool and all difficulties
increase by one. In other words, if he doesn't have two dots,
chances are the player will get annoyed after his third crash
in two sessions.

No player wants to make up a useless character. If you
know that the werewolf-to-be doesn't have many useful
Abilities, tell the player and let her create another charac-
ter. However, remember that the type of character that the
player designs shows you what sort of game she wants or
expects. A player who puts all of her points into combat
abilities wants to fight. A player who buys Computer,
Science and Repair skills won't enjoy a chronicle about a
trek across Siberia. If most or all of your players create
concepts that don't fit your chronicle, then you may want

to rethink your initial concept in the best interests of the
group. There doesn't have to be anything wrong with a
game concept for it not to work with a particular group. A
kill-fest slasher chronicle with lots of player character
death is not going to go over well witha troupe that lives for
emotional scenes and long-term character development.
To be successful, you must tailor your chronicle to suit your
players.

Also, characters with different Traits give each player a
chance to stand in the spotlight. As a general guideline,
don't let two players play characters with the same breed,
auspice, tribe and similar Abilities. They will spend the
entire chronicle battling for the same chances to shine. If
you happen to allow two players to do this, then make sure
the characters have different personalities. Usually, it just
works better to either make all players different or to share
only a tribe, breed or auspice. In a chronicle about a pack
of Fianna, each player would look to his auspice and breed
for individuality. A chronicle about a pack of metis might
produce tight relationships between characters struggling
against a society that refuses to accept them.

Remember, no matter what your players want, you have
tobe happy running the game. Many chronicles die because
the Storyteller doesn't want to keep running. If your group
doesn't want to play the type of chronicle you feel comfort-
able running, don't play. The purpose of Werewolf is
entertainment. Everyone, especially the Storyteller (who
has to do the most work), should have a good time.

The Wastes of Thule: Players

Allofhis players come up with concepts independently,
but Art gets lucky. One of his players, Lucian, creates a
former British Commando turned professor. Another player,
Courtney, makes up a graduate student and a third player,
Clark, creates an Oxford undergrad attending. The fourth
player, Brian, wants to have a wealthy young nightclub
owner and musician. Art decides to place the group in
Oxford instead of London. Lucian, Courtney and Clark are
easily located at the university. Brian's pub lands just off
campus.

However, Art does have a problem. Lucian, who's never
played Werewolf before, wants to have an older character
who served in the Falklands War. Art likes the idea of
Lucian's character serving in the Falklands, so close to
Antarctica. Art flips through the rulebook and thinks for a
while. What if the player characters all become werewolves
right at the beginning of play? Would that unbalance the
game?

Art decides to make all the characters lost children of
Gaia — Kinfolk with the potential to be werewolves who
for some reason didn't experience the Change. Now, be-
cause of the threat of the Apocalypse, the werewolf within
each one of them awakens. Art goes beyond the normal
setting to make his players happy and his story work.

Introducing Players to Werewolf

Recruit new players. Werewolf: The Apocalypse is a
great introduction to roleplaying. The game mechanics
are easy to understand; everyone has seen a | to 5 star
rating system. The World of Darkness is similar enough
to our own that a new player can draw on his own
experiences to understand the environment.

When teaching someone Werewolf, you might start
with a basic solo session, perhaps their character's Pre-
lude. This lets the new player get their feet wet and ask
all the questions they want without interrupting (and
sometimes annoying) an experienced group. New play-
ers usually do better joining a Werewolf chronicle at the
beginning.

Some Storytellers may let a new player join the game
but not allow them to read Werewolf. Instead the
Storyteller introduces them slowly to the strange world
of the Garou, and lets them learn about the environment
through interacting with it. Players experience what it is
to be a werewolf firsthand and without preconceived
notions. The sense of discovery is a feeling that can't be
faked. Even "normal" Garou abilities, such as regenera-
tion, can have a special sense of wonder attached to
them.

Asan added note, if you try this method, be careful to
take it very slowly. Hitting someone with too much
confusing stuff at once just frustrates them.

Fleshing Out the Basics

Entropy is a logical procession toward the simplest, the most
ordered state. So is drama.*

* I know the dictionary defines entropy as a progression
toward the most disordered state — but on this point, I take issue
with that most excellent book.

— David Mamet, On Directing Film
After talking to players, it's time to go back to one's notes
and make a few final decisions. Ask yourself some ques-
tions. What non player characters (NPCs) do you need to
create? Do you like all the characters in the chronicle; are
they the protagonists of your story? Will they work to-

gether? Can everyone find a good time and place to meet?
What will your story line look like?

The Outline

When designing a profluent plot, we've said, the writer works
in one of three ways, sometimes two or more at once: He
borrows some traditional story or action drawn from life; he
works backward from his climax; or he works forward from an
initial situation

— John Gardner, The Art of Fiction

Once you know your player characters and have your
theme, mood and conflicts in mind, you may then want to
sketch an outline. A good story needs an introduction, a
buildup with complications, a climax and a conclusion
The same goes for a chronicle.


|

a A

When designing a chronicle, strive at first to keep things
simple and to stay flexible. Expanding stories is far easier
than cutting them down, and there will be plenty of time to
add more to the chronicle after regular sessions start. Good
players with lots of subplots always expand chronicles more
than most Storytellers anticipate. One common mistake is
to overprepare fora story and then either having to railroad
characters through your plot without giving them choices,
or having to rework everything before each new game

session, thus putting yourself through far more work than is

necessary. When you write an outline, keep it sketchy.

Only detail things if you know your players are going to
want picky details or if it makes you feel more comfortable.

Figure out what your problems are going to be in the
game. At least a third of storytelling involves identifying

difficulties and fixing them. All chronicles have their own

ever-changing problems. A player may have bad luck with
d.
il villain and find

dice for a few weeks, and his character may get depres:

The players could all laugh at your big

her truly silly instead of truly horrific. You may set up a big
raid on Pentex for your players and build it up so much that
they get too scared to go through with it.

Cover all the bases you can. If you aren't good with on
the spot names, buy a naming-your-baby book and keep it
beside you during sessions. If you can't remember a certain
player character's background, ask fora short write-up from
all your players. Make sure you can pronounce the names of
all the Black Spiral Dancers. Think about what you will do
if the vampire manages to Blood Bond a pack member.
Make sure your NPCs have goals of their own and that
events in the chronicle make narrative sense.

Also, don't forget about your theme and mood. Every
time you design a story make sure that it stays consistent
with the overall theme and mood of your chronicle. This
doesn't mean that a tragic chronicle can't have an amusing
story. What it does mean is that radical shifts in theme
usually make a chronicle feel less coherent

Introduction:

The two most important parts of a Werewolf chronicle
are the beginning and the end. Without a solid start, the
chronicle won't last long enough to finish. Without a
strong ending, the players will sense that there was no
direction to the stories.

Introductory stories in a Werewolf chronicle set the
stage for things to come. Players should get plenty of
opportunities to explore the world and their characters'

abilities. They must meet the majot NPCs. Doing things
such as sending characters for a jaunt into the Umbra also
helps.

Introductions are the Storyteller's time to experiment
and do some testing of the group. What happens when your
Garou meet a Pattern Spider? What do they think of other
breeds or tribes? Are you and your players familiar with the
characters' Gifts? What about the combat system? What is
Garou society like in the local area? What are the major
threats? The introduction provides an opportunity for the

players and Storyteller to grow comfortable with Werewolf.
This way, a major scene isn't ruined later. For example, a
Storyteller kills off the sept's Master of the Challenge to
start a fight between the players' pack and a rival pack, only
to find out that the characters all hated the Master of the
Challenge and admire their rivals for offing her. The gist of
a few introductory stories is to gradually ease the players
into the World of Darkness.

A Storyteller should list the things that she wants to
have characters do during the chronicle's introduction
Going through a Rite of Passage or fighting ill-prepared
werewolf hunters works well. Sometimes treating players to
some success during the introduction helps give them
confidence later or makes future failure that much more
bitter. Foreshadowing and using symbolism lends itself to
introductory stories, because player characters tend to pay
more attention to the scenery at the start of a chronicle.
Write down some tentative titles and goals for the first
couple of stories. Try to have at least one story emphasizing
action and one emphasizing character development.

The introduction ends with the start of the main story
line of the chronicle. This should happen as soon as
everyone seems comfortable with the game environment.

Climax and Conclusion:

The finale is the next part of the chronicle to think
about. Some authors start work on their novels by writing
the last chapter first. They do this is to give their writing a
direction. By the same token, a Storyteller should know
where his chronicle ends.

Chapter One: Storytelling 15


etre SEES TERT SE A er A nc sear RE

Having an image of a climax and conclusion to the
chronicle doesn't mean that the players lose their freedom
of choice. A Storyteller should have a grasp on the final
scene, so she can organize the stories. This way, as the
chronicle progresses, the players can feel the buildup. The
Storyteller can drop hints about the end of the chronicle
and give Theurges visions of the future.

Decide what has to happen for the chronicle to end.
What antagonist or cataclysm waits at the end? What
elements do you need to keep in mind as you build the
chronicle?

However, don't predetermine the outcome of the final
conflict. There should be a chance for failure (or at least,
the players should believe that). Ifa Storyteller doesn't give
the players a sense that they can fail, they lose some of the
thrill of the game. Players don't have to win for a good
chronicle to be a success (but it certainly helps).

Body:

The body of the chronicle takes the pack from the
introduction to the conclusion. This part of the chronicle
should stay largely undefined. However, the Storyteller
needs to know what basic goals the pack has to achieve in
these stories in order to bring the chronicle to a satisfying
end.

A sketchy outline of these stories helps. Listing the
purpose of each story, defining its conflict, theme and mood
— provides a focus for the Storyteller when she thinks
about the future of her chronicle. Werewolf Storytellers
also need to decide on the phase of the moon and what
opportunities the characters will have to gain specific
Renown; a Philodox character has to get a chance to gain
Honor. Some Storytellers may write down at the outset
every single story they plan to run during the chronicle.
Others just make a few notes and create each story before
they start to run.

There are two important factors to remember. First, a
good Storyteller communicates with his players and adjusts
to his players' desires. This doesn't mean that if a player
wants his character to have a powerful Klaive that the
Storyteller gives it to him. Ifa player isn't having fun, then
the Storyteller takes steps to make the gaming experience
more enjoyable for him, as long as its not at the expense of
other players. Second, pacing is important. Too many
combat-oriented stories or too many intense roleplaying
stories may bog the chronicle down. Chronicles work best
when they stay in motion and have a variety of stories.

The Wastes of Thule: Introduction,
Body and Climax

Art goes back to his chronicle notes. He makes some
changes after talking to his players. Now, he has a lost-
children idea to untangle. He doesn't know where to begin,
so he skips that problem for now and moves on to other
parts of his chronicle.

Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

Art looks at his brainstorm paper and notices "Powerful
corporations" and "Secret societies" listed together. He
wants a secret society of Wyrm worshippers as antagonists.
Art decides that this secret society could be incredibly
powerful, controlling large segments of Pentex and includ-
ing Black Spiral Dancers in their membership. He places
their headquarters in London, while making a note to
himself to do some research on occult societies.

Art has a thought about this society. What if they've
killed almost all the Garou around Oxford? Art decides to
do it. The Garou around Oxford die with the stroke of the
Storyteller's pen.

Art can explain the lost-children motif if the other
Garou are dead. He considers creating an NPC who per-
formed an ancient rite to awaken lost children because of
this crisis. Or, perhaps to avenge her fallen children Gaia
has brought out the werewolf heritage within the player
characters. He thinks that either direction would work. Art
writes a note to himself to test both concepts as rumors or
theories during the introductory stories of the chronicle.
Whatever the players like becomes gospel, but they don't
need to know that.

Art decides to approach his chronicle from a character
driven angle. He doesn't yet have enough ofa handle on the
secret society, so if he focuses on the characters, he will
have more time to develop his villains. He wants the
characters to travel to Antarctica by the chronicle's end.
Art comes up with the idea that his secret society, which he
named the Dragon Club, will perform a ritual at Mount
Erebus in Antarctica, which rite will summon the Wyrm to
the real world and start the Apocalypse.

Because the player characters are lost children and the
other English werewolves are dead, even if the characters
find out the society's plans, other Garou might not listen to
them. This way, Art doesn't have to worry about his players
assembling an army of NPC Garou for his chronicle finale.
However, he realizes that he can't have all the NPCs ignore
them; otherwise, the players might decide that all other
werewolves are stupid or something of the sort, and be-
comes isolated. He makes a note to himself to be careful
about NPCs.

Art goes over his introduction. He'll begin the chronicle
by having surviving Garou find the player characters and
gather them together. Then, they will undergo an easy Rite
of Passage in the Umbra and choose a Pack Totem. The
introduction will end when the characters decide to attack
the secret society or when they do something to shred the
Veil and alert the antagonists to their presence.

For the conclusion in the Antarctic, Art ponders his
chronicle and devices some more. He decides that instead
of having his society kill most of the werewolves in En-
gland, the Dragon Club will capture and imprison them.
These Garou are drugged heavily and taken to Antarctica.
There, the society will ritually sacrifice them to awaken the
Wyrm.


To fill the body of his chronicle, Art decides to have the
characters attack one of the corporations allied with the
Dragon Club. The pack releases a captured Garou and
learns about the spirit of an abandoned caern, a spirit that
supposedly knows the secret of their transformations. Art
will have the pack slowly learn more and more about the
Dragon Club. Eventually, the characters will attack the
Dragon Club headquarters, only to discover that its leader-
ship has used Black Spiral Moon Bridge to go to Antarctica.
The pack travels through toa caern in the Antarctic where
they battle a few Black Spirals. Then, to stop the ritual they
must follow the trail of the Dragon Club members to Mount
Erebus and release the other Garou. Art will provide the
player characters with any fetishes he thinks they need
during the course of the chronicle.

Art makes a note to himself that if Antarctica doesn't
work out (the players don't like it for some reason), he'll use
asurreal place in the Umbra for Thule. He also makes some
notes next to his stories, including whether they are role-
playing oriented or more action oriented, the phase of the
moon, what type of Renown players will get and which
characters should have a chance to shine. He tries to
balance everything as much as possible, knowing that he
can change it all later.

After creating a few NPCs, Art is ready to begin his
chronicle, which he entitles The Wastes of Thule.

Roleplaying Preludes

(Optional)

Instead of running a player character's prelude as a
question and answer period with a few small roleplaying
segments, a Storyteller may wish to run a solo session with
each player to roleplay through the preludes. This may be
done immediately after each character's Traits are deter-
mined or may wait until the chronicle outline is complete.

The advantage to roleplaying a prelude is that it lets both
the Storyteller and player become familiar with the new
Garou character by giving the player lots of roleplaying
opportunities. The Storyteller gains a practical perspective
onhow the player intends to run her werewolf. By roleplaying
the major events inacharacter's background, the character's
personality develops more depth, and subplots start form-
ing even before the chronicle. This way, during the first
group session, the Storyteller has a grasp on each character.

The real disadvantage to roleplaying each prelude is
finding the time for solo sessions. If scheduling delays a
chronicle's start for too long, interest in the chronicle will
wane and players who participated in the first prelude
sessions may lose their initial feel for the character's person-
ality. In this case, the Storyteller may want to run an
introductory story before the main chronicle starts. The
players might form a pack and gain a Pack Totem. Watch-
ing players choose a Pack Totem to represent them all says
volumes about future games.

17

By roleplaying preludes before designing the bulk of the
chronicle, a Storyteller can take his players into greater
account. He has a good idea how each character works, and
he should have material for subplots.

If a Storyteller runs preludes after doing basic chronicle
design, he can start the chronicle in each prelude. Major
turning points can be foreshadowed from the start. If a
Storyteller runs preludes after sketching out the chronicle,
he should review his outline and make any needed changes.

Stories in Motion

Itis the pursuit of an ideal that is important. This pursuit will
lead to a greater possibility of the unconscious asserting itself,
which is to say, the greater possibility of beauty in your work.
The Navahos, | am told, used to weave flaws into their blankets
to let the devils out.

— David Mamet, On Directing Film

Once the troupe meets ona regular basis, the Storyteller's
real work starts. There are a few challenges for the Story-
teller. First, he needs to make sure that he keeps his
enjoyment of the chronicle and runs games consistently.
Second, he must communicate with the players and make
sure they are having a good time, all the while making sure
that all the plot lines run smoothly. Third, he needs to
continually assess and shape his chronicle, cutout plot lines
that don't work and flesh out events or subplots that play
generates, staying on top of the rules, etc.

Storytelling is easy when everyone has a good time.
When asession goes poorly, for whatever reason, storytelling
gets much harder. In the course of a long chronicle, players
will have bad sessions and so will Storytellers. Mistakes are
made. The difference between good Storytellers and me-
diocre ones is how they handle problems. Many times the
best way to deal with the difficulties is to meet them head
on and muddle through them to better sessions ahead.
Other times, a Storyteller needs a few weeks away from a
chronicle to get her creative juices flowing. Just make sure
you're having a good time. That's the most important rule
of Werewolf.

Communicating with players is an acquired skill. Some
groups have a question and answer segment at the end of
each play session or story. The Storyteller asks the players'
opinions about the events of the evening, what things they
would have done differently, what they think is going to
happen next, which NPCs they like, etc. Careful question-
ing can reveal much about how things are going. If all the
players complain about how often they frenzy, then maybe
the Storyteller needs to lower the frequency of Rage rolls.
Of course, if the chronicle focuses on the problems Garou
have with anger, then everything is going fine. A few
Storytellers promote discussions within the troupe by ask-
ing players about each other's characters.

During play, a Storyteller should watch how characters
interact with each other and what directions the pack likes
to take. If the pack prefers to use subterfuge and stealth

18 Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

a in i a a //

One problem in many roleplaying games is group
unity. One player decides that his character doesn't like
or want to do what the other characters are doing. In
Werewolf, the pack reduces this problem. Garou society
looks on a pack of werewolves as a unit. They are judged
by the actions of their packmates. Spiritually, they are
tied through their Pack Totem.

A few ways to promote these Garou feelings of pack
unity are to give characters increased attack bonuses for
working together, and to allow players to give advice to
packmates, even when their characters aren't present in
ascene. Wolf packs often act as though they were of one
mind. Why shouldn't Garou packs? The latter sugges-
tion keeps every player involved during the inevitable
times when a group splits up. Also, it prevents character
intrigue from building as much as it does in other games
such as Vampire.

This doesn't mean that conflicts won't arise. As
characters identify themselves with the pack, they will
relish the group's successes, but like a sports team, plenty
of blame will be thrown around when they lose.

The pack gives a Werewolf Storyteller a unique tool
to motivate players and hold a chronicle together. Use it
whenever possible.

instead of brute force to handle situations, the Storyteller
should know and make her plans accordingly.

She should listen when players discuss what they think
the plot lines are. As a general rule, the Storyteller should
never create an incongruous situation but should strive to
create the unexpected. Don't do the obvious, such as
making the Black Spiral Ahroun into the major villain.
Instead, make her husband, the esteemed businessman and
Kinfolk, the true manipulator of the Wyrm's plans. The
spiritual threat to the city in the Penumbra isn't a Bane

Incarna of the Wyrm , as the Garou assumed, but rather a
berserk manifestation of the Wyld. Try to avoid twisting
the plot in the middle of a session (especially if your poker
face is poor). Make changes between sessions, so you have
time to prepare and drop hints. Also, don't always penalize
intuitive players by reshaping the plot when they figure it
out. Attention to the game and creative problem solving
deserves a reward, as long as it doesn't destroy the story.

Finally, strive for consistentcy. If you make a mistake, it
usually helps to admit it to your players. If they give you a
hard time about a specific rule, take a break from the game
and ask for suggestions on how to interpret it. Perhaps your
group can come up with an idea that makes the game flow
better.

Keep track of any details that the players deem impor-
tant. Notes about the graffiti in a Bone Gnawer cub's
favorite alley may seem insignificant, but they add to the
flavor of the chronicle when the players notice new sayings


or pictures added to the wall. This way, when a player
mentions a name to you as Storyteller, you don't wonder
who or what they are talking about. Furthermore, if players
show enough interest in something minor, you can always
develop it into something more important, whether it's a
location or an NPC.

The Aftermath

Let finished chronicles stay finished. Attempts to resur-
rect good stories rarely work. Just look at the movie industry
and the sequels to any number of films. One of my favorite
comic books once killed offa major character, setting a high
standard of storytelling, only to have that same character
turn up alive several years later. The character was shuttled
through a series of highly implausible events, so the pub-
lisher could launch a new book. They lost a lot of artistic
integrity. Don't do it. There are always new chronicles and
new characters yet to play. The best games are always yet to
come.

Meaning and Myth

From Hans Christian Andersen to Carl Gustav Jung to
Joseph Campbell, authors and scholars have written about
the concepts and symbols which seem basic to the human
spirit. Although every culture has its unique spin on the
nature of reality and how humans fit into that reality, there
seem to be certain stories, certain myths, which resonate in
the hearts of all humans. The trappings may change dra-
matically from story to story, century tocentur, and country
to country, but the underlying pattern and meaning re-
mains remarkably similar. Star Wars and "Saint George
and the Dragon" are both stories revolving around the
mythic theme known as the Hero's Journey. One story may
contain lasers and Luke Skywalker and the other swords
and a knight, but the heart and spirit of the stories are
strikingly similar. Both Luke and Saint George are on the
Hero's Journey.

Werewolf Storytellers (following in the footsteps of all
the world's storytellers) can make use of these archetypal
themes in their stories and chronicles, using the basic
structure and rhythms to sculpt dramas which will keep
their players involved on more than a superficial level.
Although kicking some Pentex ass can certainly be an
enjoyable way to while away an evening or two, many

Chapter One: Storytelling

ELLE LL DL TY

a 2 a

players and Storytellers wish to add greater meaning to the
adventures of their Garou warriors. Understanding the
underlying structures of some of our species' basic stories,
our mythic cycles and our folktales can aid the Storyteller
in creating more meaningful, and thus more involving,
stories.

When a Cave Js More Than a Cave

Wherever the poetry of myth is interpreted as biography,
history, or science, it is killed. The living images become only
remote facts of a distant time or sky. Furthermore, it is never
difficult to demonstrate that as science and history mythology is
absurd. When a civilization begins to reinterpret its mythology
in this way, the life goes out of it...

— Joseph Campbell, The Hero With A Thousand Faces

Although mythic cycle stories are, in essence, very
simple stories, they have a magical quality that lifts them
above the level of a generic after school special on TV. In
order to create a story that has underlying import, both the
story and those participating in it must be greater than their
surfaces. Fairy tales, myths and folklore all provide wonder-
ful examples of how items and people can be used as
powerful symbols of even larger concepts.

When a hero in a story of mythic import enters a cave,
the cave must be more than just a hole in the side of a
mountain. Ina mythic tale the cave can stand for the hero's
own unconscious, the border between normal and magical
reality, a return to the womb, etc. What is important is that
the Storyteller knows the cave is more than just a cave; she
hopes her players will intuit at least some of this knowledge,
and thus realize that entering the cave is an extraordinary
act in and of itself.

The mythic stories common to all cultures are built upon
a foundation of symbols. The language of symbolism calls
to us on a primal level, encompassing whole areas of
experience in a single cave, or crown or heart. Concomi-
tantly, actions can also be symbolic. By using the cave as
more than a hole in a mountain the Storyteller has cast her
own building block of myth. Entering the cave is symbolic
of a willingness to explore the unknown, to enter a realm of
uncertainty from which the adventurer — you — will
emerge changed, if you return at all. As Joseph Campbell
has written: "The folk tale is the primer of the picture-
language of the soul."

The psychologist Carl Jung wrote of the collective un-
conscious, a vast sea of experience shared by all humans.
Within this deep wellspring of the human condition are the
symbols and concepts that all humans instinctively under-
stand on some level beyond and beneath all words. The
Storyteller can take advantage of our instinctive under-
standing of the language of symbolism to create richer
chronicles. Even more importantly, the Storyteller and his
players can gradually build their own collection of symbols
which have importance to their group. Such personal
symbols help to create a shared history and mythology that

I9


a A

can become even more powerful because of their intimacy
and immediacy to the players. Each time a symbol is used
within a group, its power over the imaginations and pas-
sions of the group's members grows through this
reinforcement. The object or action becomes greater than

itself; it becomes sacred.

In the World of Darkness the Garou have their own
symbols, and they recount legends to teach their pups these
symbols. This shared mythology helps keep a small, out-
sider culture like the Garou cohesive in the face of outside
pressures, Of course, the Garou have slowly begun to forget
the old tales and the old legends, and with each story
forgotten, each tale lost, the Garou move that much closer

to their own destruction.

Mythic Milestones

There is no form that is not content, no content that is not form.
—Robert Duncan, The Ideas of the Meaning of Form

In order to create stories based on universal mythic forms,
the Storyteller must first understand the symbolic steps, the
milestones generally traversed by the protagonists (the player
characters) during such a story. These milestones are the
events, both symbolic and actual, which occur during the
course of a mythic tale.

The steps outlined below describe the flow of a typical
mythic story. However, Storytellers should feel free to play
with this form and use any information they find helpful in
creating their own mythic forms. Remember though, that the
people, places and events you choose for a particular story
should always be more than just themselves: they should stand
forsomething greater. Without this element of the sacred, the
story cannotattain the intensity and importance of myth. This
mythic (or folkloric) quality is most easily achieved when the
Storyteller first determines the theme he wishes to convey in
hisstory. The particular forms the plot elements then take may
all be chosen for theirability to dramatize a theme tothe Garou
heroes.

Call of the Wyla

Regardless of the type of adventure on which the players
embark, they must somehow take the first steps on their
quest. This "call to adventure," as Joseph Campbell terms
it, can take any number of forms: from an eager pack of
nt Garou venturing out on their Rite of Passage to
a lone warrior suddenly kidnapped by a roving band of
faeries. The call to adventure, voluntary or involuntary,

adoles

always heralds a transforming adventure. Often, there is
some dramatic event which tempts the heroes into inves-
tigating, or otherwise heralds the start of the adventure.

Into the Dark

Once the heroes have embarked on their adventure,
they must reach a point where they stand between two
worlds. Here, they are at the brink of true adventure, for
they have reached a precipice between the known world of

2 eres nares rea care eri rrr A nner mna ree)

\


comfortable certainties and the vast unknown beyond. The
first of these two worlds or conditions, is almost always the
realm of the heroes' waking, everyday reality. It is the grind
of school, hunting with the pack, the passing of waking
hours.

The second condition, however, is less defined. No
matter what it is, though, it isa condition markedly differ-
ent from the world in which the players normally live. In
stepping from the first world to the second, the players
venture into the realm of true adventure. Symbolically,
they plunge into the deep waters of the collective uncon-
scious, the Dreamtime, the Umbra. This second,
transformative world may be anything from the land of
dreams (and the Garou's own unconscious mind) to a
Pentex facility so vile it personifies some facet of universal
atrocities, some aspect of the Wyrm itself.

Guardian at the Gate

It isnever as simple to step between these two realities as
it might at first seem. In myths there is always a guardian
blocking the way of any would-be heroes who wish to pass.
To prove their worth, the characters must somehow defeat
or bypass this guardian. Depending on the nature of the
tale, the guardian can take forms ranging from a phobia
which must be conquered to a pack of Black Spiral Dancers
slavering like Cerberus itself for the taste of the blood of
their estranged brethren.

The method required to win past this guardian also
varies with the type of story and the choices of both players
and Storyteller. Solutions ranging from violence to flattery
may be used effectively, depending on the guardians. Very
often, the form the guardian takes and the most successful
methods to win past it are important symbolic clues to the
nature of the adventure itself.

The Transfiguration

At this point in the story the protagonists face their
ultimate challenge. If they are successful, they receive the
reward they set out to gain. This reward might be knowl-
edge, a powerful fetish, the release of some curse, or a
dramatic change in their world view. Sometimes the re-
ward received does not seem positive at all. Yet it may
reveal something of great import to the protagonists, just as
a scab ripped from an infected wound reveals the dirt and
pus beneath. The discovery is repulsive, but once the
infection is revealed it can truly be healed at last.

The Journey Home

Once the protagonists have achieved their goal, they
must begin the journey back to their own reality. This
journey can be extremely simple, a triumphant return to
the fold, or it can be a mad flight out of darkness with the
forces of the Wyrm breathing hot upon the protagonists'
heels. The nature of the journey home may well depend on
both the type of journey (see below) as well as the measure
of success.

Chapter One: Storytelling

a 2 a

Rejoining the Pack

At the end of the journey home, the protagonists must
once again cross the threshold between the two disparate
realities. This time, however, they are crossing from the
magical, transformative realm to the solidity of the every-
day world. As with the initial crossing, there is often a price
that must be paid or a struggle overcome for the protago-
nists to win their way back to their own world.

The rejoining of the pack is of particular importance to
the Garou. For most Garou, the pack is the mainstay, the
basic truth around which their lives revolve. As pack
creatures, it is hard for most Garou to view anything as truly
important unless it involves the tribe, or sept or pack. The
individual Garou achieves importance through her rela-
tion to these units. When a character returns from the
realm of myth she must bring back the elixir, the knowl-
edge, etc., and present it to her people lest her entire
journey become meaningless. Just as her life only has real
value in relation to the pack, so too does her adventure only
achieve real importance when the fruits of her labors
belong to the pack. It is by risking body and soul to bring
something of importance to her people that the Garou can
become a cultural hero. Without this relationship to the
pack, the journey is meaningless. The Rite of Keres (see the
Werewolf Second Edition rulebook, p. 151) is an example
of the importance of the pack. Keres symbolically sacrifices
herself, becoming her tribe, when she eats the bodies of her
fallen packmates to keep them from being tainted by the
Wyrm.

Grials and Tribulations

Throughout the course of a mythic tale the protagonists
must be tested to prove their worth. Without this temper-
ing, this struggle, the protagonists cannot understand the
vital importance their actions will have on the world. This
series of tests can vary from one or two simple encounters
to a complex hierarchy of tests taking weeks, or even
months, to accomplish.

It is important, however, that the protagonists be pro-
foundly tested in some way. Very often these trials serve to
confront the protagonists with a facet of the evil they are
destined to face at the climax of their adventure. This
tempting, tricking, attacking and testing tempers the pro-
tagonists so that they might successfully face the final
challenge. The trials are normally designed to probe the
protagonists for any weaknesses that might ally them with
the enemy they face. If their weaknesses prove too similar
to the evil they face, the protagonists might not be able to
stand firm against their enemy. The protagonists must face
and conquer these vulnerabilities before they can complete
their appointed task.

|


ee NN

ne A Ae

Kami, Kinfolk and Kindred Spirits

In contrast to the many tests the protagonists have to
face are the helpers who often appear to provide some
support to the struggling Garou. The number and type of
helpers that come to the aid of the protagonists may vary
greatly, and such helpers do not always appear in these
stories. Even when they do appear, such helpers often at
first appear to be inconveniences or even enemies, just as
enemies may sometimes appear as friends. Things are cer-
tainly not always as they appear during the transformative,
magical period of the story. How the Garou treat such
helpers often determines the amount of assistance they
receive.

Sacred Stories

I'm hearing right and wrong so clearly

There must be more than this

It's only in uncertainty

That we're naked and alive

— Peter Gabriel, "That Voice Again"

There are a dozen, perhaps a few more, fundamental
story lines which appear over and over throughout history.
Of these, the most famous, and arguably the fundamental
story, is the Hero's Journey. The following collection of
stories (including the Hero's Journey) provide examples of
how the Storyteller can manipulate the skeletal mythic
story line given above to encompass a variety of story types.
The following plot sketches also give the Storyteller a
sampling of certain common mythic themes on which he
can hang various places, people and things to individualize
his story while still maintaining the mythic impact of the
theme.

Auspice Archetypes

For individual Garou, their auspice gives hints to the
nature and general strengths and weaknesses of character,
but auspices serve an even more valuable role for Garou as
a society. Auspices serve as a way for the Garou to ensure
that certain important functions are performed for the pack
and sept. For that reason, it is necessary that young Garou
understand the true importance and interwoven nature of
all the auspices. One of the ways to explore the importance
and nature of auspices in Garou culture is through mythic
stories.

The stories chosen give examples of mythic themes and
structures, but they are also important in that the Garou
consider these five stories to be archetypal ones for the five
auspices. Galliards, when telling the tale of a particularly
brave Garou who successfully walked his particular auspice's
archetypal path, say he has "feasted in the den of Phoebe."
Such Garou are thought to epitomize the qualities of their
auspice and are regarded with favor, normally gaining three
points of Renown in addition to any other points gained
during the quest. Ahrouns garner Glory, Galliards Wisdom

22
i ———

orGlory, Philodox gain Honor, Theurges gain Wisdom and
Ragabash gain whatever is proper to the nature of the quest.

Each of the archetypal stories below is associated with a
particular auspice's path. All Garou may embark on an
adventure of any of these types, but the Storyteller can take
advantage of the particular auspice role emphasis ina given
story to heighten an adventure's significance to one or
more of the characters involved. In addition, these stories
can emphasize the value of a particular auspice to Garou
culture. The Storyteller can give the characters such a story
because they do not seem to realize the importance of a
particular auspice (or because one member of the party does
not get to "shine" very often, and the story will give hima
good opportunity to do so). Conversely, such stories can be
used as specific, advanced Rites of Passage for Garou who
need to learn more about the mythic importance of auspices
and their auspice in particular.

The Hero's Journey (Ahroun)

The Hero's Journey is the story of personal transforma-
tion. Even more importantly, it is the story of personal
transformation which also has an impact on the world
around the hero. The most fundamental example of the
Hero's Journey is the Rite of Passage. During the course of
a Rite of Passage, a child sets out on the journey to
adulthood. If successful, the child is transformed during the
Rite of Passage and an adult returns. The Rite of Passage is
for Garou both a psychological and physical adventure
during which the child must prove to her fellow Garou, but
more importantly to herself, that she is ready to take up the
responsibilities and freedom that come with adulthood.

There are, of course, many forms of transformative
journey. Often the hero sets out on a journey on which he
plunges into the darkness of the unknown to find some
object or knowledge crucial not only to the hero, but to all
of his people. The hero risks the darkness to bring about a
revolutionary change that transforms not only himself, but
all of society.

The following are two side-by-side examples of possible
Hero's Journeys. Keep in mind that these two examples
emphasize the basic framework of the Hero's Journey, and
that Storytellers should not be limited by these examples.
It is important when creating a story or chronicle centered
on the Hero's Journey, or any of the other mythic themes
discussed here, that the Storyteller emphasize her own
chosen theme and mood.

Call of the Wyld: For the cubs undergoing a Rite of
Passage, the call to adventure is often in the form of a
kidnapping. They are dragged into a situation in which
each is forced to act heroically or forever remain a child in
the eyes of their newfound pack. When a cub takes up the
challenge under the eyes of the ritemaster and moves to
take her place among the young Garou to be tested, she has
taken her first few steps on the Hero's Journey.

The Hero's Journey may also begin with a conscious
decision to enter the magical land to discover something of

Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

a ae // -

|


great importance to both the hero and his people. For
example, several Silver Fang warriors determine to go ona
quest to discover why the energies of their sept's caern are
inexorably waning with each passing month. In making
this decision to venture into the unknown for the good of
the caern, the Garou are beginning their transformation
into true heroes.

Into the Dark: The young cubs on their Rite of Passage
set out for the abandoned factory from which, they have
been told, strangely twisted and crazed animals have
emerged. As they quietly climb the cracked loading ramp
and enter the blackened maw of the gaping doorway, they
have crossed the threshold between the known and the

unknown worlds.

Alternatively, the Silver Fangs make their way to the
heart of their caern and perform a Rite of Contrition to
purify themselves before stepping sideways into the Umbra,
moving from one world to the next.

in at the Gate: Just as the young Garou pass

snarls as several wolf-fomori (representing the weakest,
back-biting aspects of the Garou) leap upon the nervous
young werewolves. This fight will determine whether they

The intrepid Silver Fangs pause as they arrive in the

gnarled old Garou (a helper). The old one offers the Garou

as the old one begins to chew on the petals, after which he
slowly fades from view. One by one, the Garou swallow the
petals of the flowers, each feeling a hideous sensation as
their hearts seem to slow and then stop. Collapsing, they
pass into the depths of the unknown (here a pseudodeath is
the guardian to be passed).

Just as they are about to attack the Bone Gnawer-defilers

run with the Umbral Garou of all tribes, healing the
spiritual wound to the caern.

The Journey Home: The young cubs head back to their
sept, triumphant and happy. Several younger wolves ac-
company them on their journey (representing Gaia's
pleasure), and they meet no further obstacles.

Once they are prepared to journey back home the Silver
Fangs follow a gradually spiraling path through the field
until they reach a second field of flowers, this one roses.
Eating the roses, the Garou return to the physical world

Rejoining the Pack: When the young cubs return to the
sept bearing the transformed elixir, they too are trans-
formed. No longer cubs, the returning heroes are met as full
members of the sept.

The Silver Fangs return to their sept bearing important
tidings. Although it is painful news to bear for the proud
Silver Fangs, the returning heroes must tell their elders that
it is the cancer of pride within that harms the sept. Changes
must be made if the cancer is to be cured.

The Pollen Path of Beauty (Galliard)

Following the Pollen Path of Beauty isa Navaho term for
meditating upon and discerning the wisdom found in
dreams and other mystical states. The Pollen Path is the

23

path of the talesinger, the creator and the dreamer. One can
gain much wisdom along this unique and ever-shifting
path, butit is a path that requires a great flexibility of spirit
to walk successfully. Yet, the wisdom learned on this path
gains value only when shared with others. The path seldom
yields much of solitary, individual worth. Rather, the knowl-
edge comes in great, strange chunks that can be related only
in song and story, in art and dance, and only then does its
meaning become clear. The more who hear of the wisdom
found on the Pollen Path, the greater that wisdom becomes.
Thus, although few walk the path, those few bear a great
responsibility to the many.

Storytellers should feel especially free to use symbols and
folktale-type plots when using this form of story. Large,
impartial truths are explicated through the people, places
and things found on this journey. As Alice discovered
beyond the looking glass, not all is how it seems. Sleeping
insanity may reveal more to the dreamer than waking
sanity. Galliard characters should be encouraged to tell the
tale of any journey they take upon the Pollen Path. It is an
honor to Gaia, and the telling and interpreting of such tales
is one of the most important roles the Moon Dancers play
for the Garou. Any Galliard characters in such a story will
more readily understand the imagery being used, and the
Storyteller may wish to slant the story accordingly or give
the Galliard's player hints and additional information.

The following is an example ofa folklore-style story with
much symbolic importance to the Garou. The information

24
lh —_——

the Storyteller wishes to impart with this tale is that the
Black Spiral Dancers, the Garou's fellows and the shadow
that lurks within their race, have returned to the area of the
sept. Now, more than ever, the Garou must be especially
prepared for battle. (This may also be a story warning that
the Apocalypse is approaching shortly, and that the Garou
must face and purge their own evils in preparation for this
greatest of world-deaths and the following rebirth.)

Call of the Wyld: The players' Garou are hunting in the
woods near their sept when they notice a particularly
powerful and haughty buck standing at the intersection of
two animal paths. The buck stares at the group fora second
or two before contemptuously bounding off down one trail.

The buck serves as the agent to draw the Garou down the
path to adventure, while the crossroads symbolizes an
important choice.

Guardian at the Gate: As the Garou follow the buck,
they pass through some low hanging willow trees (associ-
ated with the moon) and reach the bank of a small lake with
an island at its center. Their own dark, distorted images,
reflected in the lapping water, gaze at the Garou. The buck
swims determinedly towards the island. When the Garou
enter the water they find themselves suddenly pulled un-
der. Some faerie friends of the deer wish to defend the buck,
and the Garou must somehow get past them.

The dark, distorted images are a reminder of the Black
Spirals, while crossing the water signifies a willing immer-
sion in the dream realm. The island ahead is the realm of
prophecy and dream.

The Transfiguration: When the Garou finally reach
the small island, they notice a forlorn old scarecrow still
propped up ona pole, its arms outstretched. The stag stands
behind the scarecrow, while in front of it sit a group of black
wolf cubs who bite and pick at a large turtle that has been
flipped over on its back. When the Garou move closer, the
cubs back away, and the Garou see the turtle is dead.

As the Garou watch, the stag crumples to the ground. A
wind blows across the water, and the stag's body crumbles
into ashes which fill the turtle's empty shell. Seeing this,
the wolf cubs transform into crows and fly into the trees,
where they watch.

The stag is both a representative of Gaia and a totem of
the Fianna (which may indicate that the Fianna are in
trouble or are a key to saving the Garou). The turtle
represents the lost Croatan tribe (whose totem it was), and
the scarecrow symbolizes its sacrifice. The ashes of the stag
are the ashes of atonement as well as pain (and perhaps a
sign of the coming Apocalypse). The black cubs are the
Black Spiral Dancers, and they also represent the danger of
the tribes turning against each other in times of need. The
Storyteller could use the ashes themselves as a talisman
item, or the shell as a fetish.

The Journey Home: This will depend on how the Garou
dealt with the faeries of the lake. Returning to the mainland
could well be a difficult proposition. The Storyteller may
also wish to interweave the pack's personal totem into the
