City Stories

Told my little Pollyanna,

Pollyanna come with me

We'll go down to Transverse City

Where life is cheap and death is free

Past the condensation silos

Past the all-night trauma stand

We'll be there before tomorrow

Pollyanna take my hand.

— Warren Zevon, "Transverse City"

To some Garou, cities are even more alien than the
realms within the Umbra. Worse yet, cities are strongholds
of the Wyrm and the Weaver. They teem with Pentex-
owned companies, large numbers of Leeches and foul things
unimagined. Bringing out the ugliness and horror which
cities represent to the Garou is left to the Storyteller's
manipulation. Some players may feel similarly to the Garou,
but those who live within metropolitan areas may see
things differently. Remind players they are playing Garou,
and unless they are Bone Gnawers or Glass Walkers, it is
highly unlikely that they enjoy cities. E
lived in cities before making the Change

Garou who

commonly feel
uncomfortable in them, not only because returning to the
city might conjure up memories of their former lives, but
also because of the spiritual awareness they gained after
becoming Garou. In fact, Garou born in cities can find
them even more horrifying than they are for other Garou.

Describing the City

Here area few of the elements a Storyteller might play up
when describing cities to players:

* Abandonment and Decay — Garou can see, smell and
feel the work of the Wyrm upon the Weaver's constructions
and they smell the pungent stench the minute they enter a
city. Some Garou actually smell terrible odors, but most
merely imagine they do. The streets are dirty, stained with
the filth of the humans. There are abandoned buildings,
stripped down cars, graffiti and massive city dumps.

¢ Absurdity — The city is an insane place. The laws of
nature are thrown out and replaced with stupid human-
made rules. Crosswalks, stop lights, nine-to-five jobs, and
countless carloads of humans rushing from one place to the
next. Human behavior is odd, unnatural and ultimately
confusing when Garou look for meaning within human
society.

¢ Allurement — Some Garou are naturally drawn to
cities. They fascinate them. Perhaps they are curious about
how humans live, or maybe they like technology. For
whatever reason, some Garou characters are not offended
by the city as much as their packmates are. Instead, they
enjoy the experience and see it as an adventure.

© Artificiality — Cities were created by the Weaver for
humans and urban existence itself is insane and unnatural.
There is very little ground to walk on which has not been


covered over and hidden by ment. There are few trees

and almost no wild animals. Instead, humans break the laws
of nature by making animals property, calling them pets

* Callousness — The cold ruthlessness of humans is
highly visible. No one is friendly, onlookers turn away from
cries of help from their fellow humans, strangers seldom
speak to one another and crime is rampant. Humans are
worse than animals — at least while animals have their
instincts to blame for their callousness, humans can only
blame themselves (or society, depending on whether or not
you ask a bleeding-heart liberal).

° "City-Sicknes
ill when they remain in the city for long periods of time

— Some Garou become physically

Without the wide-open wilderness to roam in, their muscles
atrophy, their senses dull, and their predator instincts are
either lost or they become overwhelming. Some constantly
feel dirty while others feel empty inside. There is no one

single "city-sickness," as each Garou is affected differently.

In fact, some may eventually become weak or even bedrid-
den from this separation from their home. The worst part is
their separation from nature, from Gaia. It is not known

Fie SPOOR OF4

whether the sickness is psychosomatic or spiritual in na-
ture, but to most Garou the reason does not matter.

* City Slickers and Country Bumpkins — The Garou
of the wilds are usually contemptuous of the city Garou
because they live in the stronghold of the Weaver and
Wyrm. However, the Garou of the city are often equally
discourteous to their country kin. Glass Walkers and Bone
Gnawers often see country Garou as naive, ignorant and
outdated. Interaction between the two groups is often terse
and abrasive.

© City Spirits — The Penumbra of the city is a strange
place. Stepping sideways is hard due to the high Gauntlet,
and once on the other side the problems are not over, as
there are countless Pattern Spiders crawling about. The
Umbrascapes of cities are filled with intricate, interweav-
ing collections of webs dwarfing the Wyld's minuscule
influence

* Corruption and Violence — Corruption is every-
where. Violence is everywhere. But in the city, things are
far worse. In the cities, the cruelest of the cruel prey on the
weak, and the actions of Leeches can be child's play

66 Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

[fT eT ee 7,

|

compared to some human depravities. It is obvious that
human society is sick. Government, the Weaver's grand
creation to be imposed on the lives of humans, is breaking
down because of the Wyrm's corruption. Human leaders
have become pawns to Leeches and corporations. Most of
the citizenry are mindless cattle who want to be controlled.
Garou, like many humans, are sickened by the monster
society has become.

© Filth — For the most part, Garou are clean creatures.
Cities are filthy and crawl with diseased humans, the
homeless, whores and others whom most Garou would
consider unclean. Garou remember the cities of centuries
ier

ago through their past lives, cities which were far nas
than they are nowadays, and the imagery affects their own
view of cities in the present. In fact, the Bone Gnawers
epitomize filth to most other Garou, which is why they are
avoided by most.

¢ Immensity — Skyscrapers and other cyclopean struc
tures are often intimidating to Garou. Standing below such
Weaver-born constructions can cause a Garou feelings of
despair or even Harano. Most Garou consider the Wyrm
their primary foe and do not realize how powerful the
Weaver is until they stand in its shadow.

¢ Irritation — After being immersed ina sea of humans
for a long period of time, Garou often feel edgy. Though
there is no hunting ground, the predator-instincts still
come out and Garou begin to desire feeding upon humans.
Even homid Garou begin thinking of people as potential
food, whether they want to or not. Should they frenzy in the
presence of humans, it is likely they will attempt to feed on
them.

* Lack of Privacy Being totally alone in the
overcrowded city is difficult. The homeless line the alley-
ways, the criminals haunt the seemingly abandoned streets,
and even in one's own home, humans are always no more
than 200 feet way. Garouare sensitive to this. The Impergium
was designed to keep human populations within an accept-
able limit. The Impergium failed and now cities are centers
of humanity's increasing tide. Therefore Garou often look
at cities as symbols of their failure.

© Loneliness — Even Garou recognize the loneliness of
living in the city. They are forever surrounded by strangers
when out in public. Though most humans are comfortable
with this, Garou of the country are used to being among the
others they know. There is no real sense of community in
the city. Even some of the city septs are more like business
organizations than they are extended families.

All humans are selfish. They work to
reap pleasure and power, and they use everyone and every-

¢ Narcissism —

thing they can to get what they want. Worse than humans,

Leeches are professional narcissists, spending endless cen-
turies serving their own vanity and working towards their
selfish goals. Even feeding off humans for their own survival
is a symbol of how they put themselves above all others.
¢ Paranoia — Most Garou feel as though they are being

watched by the totem spirit of a city. In fact, they usually

are. Just about every city has a totem spirit and the spiritcan

know anything that goes on in the city. The totem keeps
careful watch over the Garou and even serves as a Pack
Totem to some. Out-of-town Garou often get the feeling
they are being watched every once in a while. Those who
are especially sensitive or paranoid often feel they are
watched the whole time they are in the city.

© Perversity — Garou have their own morality, but
they share many views in common with humans (as they
can take human form and do think like them to some
degree), Garou are put off by the deviant behavior which is
so commonplace within large cities. Most Garou see Goth
culture (acting like Leeches), medicine and drug use (put-
ting something foreign in the body tochange it), molestation
(unnatural), and greed (corruption), among other things,
ions of the Wyrm.

as expres

* The Rich and the Poor — The gap between the social
s is most visible in the city. Garou notice this and are
offended by it. Even Garou of the lowest rank are treated

classe'

with at least some respect. Personal value is based on the
quality of the individual not the size of their bank account.

© Sensory Overload —
outside the city may be overwhelmed by the constant

Garou who normally live

barrage of sights, smells and sounds, becoming confused
and irritated from the sensory overload. They may even
suffer headaches, earaches or similar problems until they
learn to handle all the sensory input.

© Surrounded by the Leeches — Homid Garou born in
the city are especially sensitive to the fact that vampires
have always lived in cities, moving through human society
with ease. Most might even realize they have probably seen
a vampire or two, maybe even have spoken to them,
without even knowing it. Worse yet, a homid Garou might

realize Mr. Witherspoon down the street never seems to get
any older and never comes out of his apartment during the
day. Another might realize that cousin Emma sure does give
off some strange vibes and does look a little peaked now that
you mention it.

© Surrounded by the Weaver — Garou who travel a
city's Penumbra are often overwhelmed by the presence of
the Weaver. Pattern Spiders are everywhere weaving their
master's webs, while slave elementals do the bidding of
their masters. The Weaver can be felt everywhere and in
everything within a city. Garou find being in the lair of
their enemy most unnerving.

* Surrounded by the Wyrm — Just as the Weaver is
strong, so is the Wyrm. Its presence can be felt in nearly
everything and everyone within a city. Humans are pro-
grammed to believe the world is their property and cities
only amplify this attitude, which greatly disgusts Garou.

© Technology — Technomancer mage handiwork is
most visible in the city and science is merely a clever hand
puppet of the Weaver. It makes life too easy and weakens
those who become dependent upon it. Garou of the country
see Glass Walkers, who are so in tune with technology, as
part of the problem, not part of the solution.

Reasons to Go to the City

Sometimes a Storyteller needs to come up with a good
reason for getting Garou characters, who normally reside in
the country, into the city. Here area few possible reasons for
Garou characters to end up in the city.

¢ Accidentally —Garou sometimes travel by car through
a city and sometimes the cars break down, Sometimes
Garou step sideways back from the Umbra to find them-
selves (if the player rolled a botch and the Storyteller is
feeling particularly nasty) right in the middle of the city,
miles away from where they should be. Mages, as well, may
cause strange "coincidental" events which can cause Garou

to end up in the city.

¢ A Chase Into the City
get chased into the city or they might chase an enemy into
the city. The latter is the more plausible of the two, but

Either the characters could

either is possible. Sabbat and Gangrel vampires often roam
about the wilderness and Garou stupid enough to follow
these creatures into the city usually get what they deserve.

¢ A City Moot —

septs within the city and those outside, moots are held in

In maintaining relations between

the city and Garou from outside areas are invited to attend.

¢ Fighting the Good Fight — Many enemies exist
within the city: Net Spiders, fomori, Black Spiral Dancers,
Wyrm-creatures of the sewers, Pentex, vampires, mage:

mad scientists, werewolf hunters and the Seventh Genera-
tion: There are undoubtedly other antagonists a devious
Storyteller could work in. The bad thing about the situa-
tion is that the Garou must fight within their enemy's
territory, putting werewolves at a disadvantage.

* To Find Something Important — Sometimes Garou

must go into the city in search of something important (of

the Storyteller's choosing). It could be information, a
person, or an object. The characters might even be there to
rescue someone. They could be in the city to deal with an
old enemy or even to find someone to train them in a
particular Ability or Gift.

© To Guard Something — Garou must sometimes go to
the city to guard something or someone located there. The
characters might help out another sept or another ally by
serving as a guard over something. The characters could be
envoys carrying something important to (or bringing back
something from) someone in the city. The characters may
be guarding a place, a person or some other object, possibly
even a spirit or one of their former enemies.

© Hunting — Some Red Talons and other lupus Garou
who enjoy slaughtering humans actually conduct their own
little personal Impergiums within cities. Such actions never
end happily, as Leeches, hunters, and Wyrm-minions are
drawn to these hunters of humans.

* Kidnapping or Capture — The characters could get
captured or kidnapped by their enemies or someone who
plans on using them. For example, the vampiric sect known
as the Sabbat routinely conducts "Fox Hunts" in which a
single' Garot is captured and turned loose in the city. All

68

the Leeches then hunt the Garou down to slay it before it
can escape. In another example, a rival sept captures the
pack, hoping they will join their rival's sept. The other
Garou may, possibly, use threats, blackmail or similar
means to coerce the characters.

* A Meeting — The Garou must often meet with their
Allies, Kinfolk, and Contacts within the city. Only a few
who know of the Garou put themselves at risk by going into
their territory. Garou occasionally meet with vampires,
mages, and others supernatural beings who live in the city
to challenge one another's actions, form alliances, trade
information or just talk. Most of these meetings take place
in the suburbs, as neither side trusts the other in their
respective territories.

¢ Personal Matte
matters: visiting the family, shopping, going to a concert,

—There are many possible personal

conducting business dealings or even sheer curiosity. Garou
often find trouble without it having to find them.

Bad City Encounters

1. The Alligators of the Sewer — Should the characters
enter the sewer, there is a chance they might encounter a
Sewer Gator, such as the one detailed in Chapter Four. This
type of encounter can highlight the unexpected and wild
aspect of a city.

2. The Bums of the Wyrm

Living in the City

A Storyteller should feel free to introduce any of the

following ill effects, which might develop result of a

wilderness-bred Garou living in the city fora long period
of time:

1. Homid Garou show an increased desire to feed on

humans, making it easier for them to fall under the Thrall
of the Wyrm

2. It is initially easier to gain Rage points, but over
time becomes harder to gain Rage.

3. It is more difficult to regain Gnosis points as the

connection to nature is lost.

4. Heightened Senses, Primal-Urge, Sense Wyrm and
other senses are dulled.

5. Growing paranoia. Treat as a Derangement if you
have Book of the Wyrm.

6. Weakening connection to the Wyld.

7. Growing connection to the Weaver or the Wyrm.

8. Garou may slowly become jaded. They will prob-
ably develop inane human-like habits as well.

9. Decrease in the Delirium effect on humans.
10. Loss of Physical Attributes over a long period of
time.

die and disappear. The Bone Gnawers do not know the
source of the problem yet, but many of them have been
accused of this vile problem and they are investigating

Some Garou might even find and accuse the characters of
breaking the Ban of Man.

3. The Shopping Mall of the Wyrm — If the characters
go shopping or pass through stores while in the Penumbra,
they will find the Wyrm's presence strong. They will find

countless Wyrm-tainted goods, which will surely repulse
them. Worse yet, some may even be Wyrm-infested, mean-
ing that in some of the products are actual Banes which
might attempt to corrupt or kill the characters.

4. The Unlucky Mugger — A mortal mugger attempts
to roba lone character. He pulls a gun on the character and
will shoot him for any aggressive resistance.

5. The Wild Dogs — At night, the characters notice
packs of wild dogs who roam the streets like wolves, scav-
enging for food and attacking lonely stragglers. One of the
dogs, perhaps the leader, isa halfbreed offspring ofadog and
a Bone Gnawer. Worse yet, she is Garou though she has
never made the Change.

6. Mortal Authorities — The police approach the
cters to arrest them, possibly even raiding wherever

are staying while in the city. The characters have been
given up to the police, either for actual crimes they have
committed in the city or on false charges. They have been
set up by Garou, mages or Leeches who consider the

Chapter Three: Setting

——

characters a threat. Should the characters fight and kill the
police, they will find themselves hunted by the authorities
even more fiercely. Worse yet, whoever is setting them up
seems to know wherever they go, so the police are always on
their tails.

7. Baby in the Dumpster — The characters find a live
human baby in a dumpster. This will probably repulse the
characters, but what are they going to do with the baby?

A City Story

You, my boy. Come here, I want to tell you my story. You

er hear it

had better listen to my tale now, for you will nev

again. A long time ago, I was not a Glass Walker, I was not
even a Theurge. | was an Ahroun of the Warpath Wendigo
I was a brave lupus who ran with my kind throughout the
forest, hunting, killing and living free off the land. But my life
among my people was innocent and empty. I could see no
We would skirmish with the Black
Spirals to the north only to suffer from their raids again after

purpose in our ways

they had recovered from our own. It seemed an endless cycle,
but it was not. It was a spiral inward to the Wyrm at the
middle and my rage came not from my own bloody wounds,
but from my anger at my people

My pack was given a mission. We were to go into the city
and kill a group of fomori. This group had killed some of our
young who were still living with their human families in the
suburbs. My pack was anxious to spill blood, but they hated
the thought of going into the city. Though it was true that
some among us had lived there as humans until discovering
our true nature, that seemed a lifetime ago, and indeed it
was. Unlike the others, I did not mind going into the city. As
amatter of fact, was rather intrigued. You see, being a lupus
raised in the wild, I had never been there before and all the
rumors and myths fascinated me. I could not wait. If pos-
sible, I would even stall the others just to give myself time to
explore

We
Chrysler Le Baron if remember correctly, into the city. We

ft the next morning and traveled by car, an old

stopped for lunch at a human restaurant which served what
the others called pizza. Even as the others seemed to enjoy the
Meat Lover's pizzas we ordered, I did not let on that I did
After all, I was the wild lupus and the leader of the pack to
boot. We spent that afternoon driving around the area where
the fomori had been spotted before, taking turns using our
power to sense Wyrm taint to locate them. It was not an easy
task, as you know. Just about everything that isn't of the
Weaver in this city is of the Wyrm. Still, by luck it seemed,
we found one of the creatures and we followed it. We were
either too excited or too stupid to realize how the odds were
against us simply stumbling upon one of the fomori and how
our good fortune must have been a setup.

Indeed, it was a setup, and before we knew what hap-
pened, they were down on us like lightning. The battle was
terrible and I was wounded, though I did not feel it. We killed
four before we realized we that we had walked into a nest of

You see, our people fear the city, for they have never seen
its heart nor its power. I have seen them both and they are the
same as those within me. My place is here. I can see what is
good in the Weaver's work. The beauty of the city lights at
night, the power of driving a sports car at 150 miles per hour,
the majesty of the CyberRealm and the glory of leading a Net
Spider attack on a Pentex office. These things most of our
kind will never know. They fear the Weaver and refuse to use
its work as their own weapons. They do not even look at
humans the way we Glass Walkers do. Prejudice clouds their
minds and portrays humans as mindless savages who care
about nothing but wealth, power and violence. They do not
look any deeper out of fear they might find their true enemies
are not unlike themselves.

I will never speak to you of this again, nor are you ever
speak of this to anyone. I have shown you my secret and you
must keep it until the day you die, else I will kill you. Besides,
my young Wendigo nephew from the forest, I can see your
heart too. And it is the same as mine. Same as this city's
You'll be back. I know you will. And I'll have a place waiting
for you when you do

Why They Love the City

The Glass Walkers and Bone Gnawers do not view the
city in the same way as do other Garou. The following
elements might be played up to Glass Walker and Bone
Gnawer characters. A crafty Storyteller might choose

one or two Garou from the country who should slowly
come to see the city in the same way as the city Garou.

© Center of Everything — The city is where the
action takes place. The city is the stronghold of the
Weaver and the Wyrm, and it will one day be the new
battleground. Even now, it is far easier to find battles to
fight and problems to solve in the city than it is in the
forest.

* City Father — Many Glass Walkers share a spiri-
tual link with the city they call home. Some even see the
city as more spiritual place than the wilderness. They are
its protectors in many ways, and in return the city
protects those of them who are strong or worthy. When
they spend time outside the city, they often feel cut off
from their spiritual side, just as do country Garou when
they spend time in the city. Also, the Garou of the city
learn to use the large number of city spirits, even those
of the Weaver, to aid them in serving the Wyld and
fighting the Wyrm.

* Curiosity — Some Garou who come to the city
become so enraptured with the exploration of it they
make it their home. Bone Gnawers enjoy exploring the
many secret and abandoned places within the city, while
others enjoy studying human culture by going to school,
visiting museums, etc. Many find the art and culture of
the humans quite beautiful.

* Intensity of Life — The rush hours, the night life,
and all the other cycles of a city that never sleeps are

Chapter Three: Setting

EE LE TY

attractive to some Garou. They can feel the pulsing
energy of the city and it can intensify their emotions,
invigorate them and keep them excited.

© Possibility — Most see the Weaver as stagnation,
but others see it as everything which can possibly exist.
The Wyld may be all possibility, but it is the Weaver
which defines it and gives it meaning. To city Garou the
diversity found within the city is wondrous. There is
everything you could ever want and it is all close by.

* Strategic Value — Only a few Garou realize the
advantage they would have if they could get a foothold
on the city. Many of those who move to the city do so for
this reason alone: it makes their fight against the Wyrm
and the Weaver much easier. Living in the city makes it
easy to learn the weaknesses of the enemy and fight them
on their own territory. As open warfare has far graver

consequences for Garou who live near their enemies,
many city Garou end up playing the power games of the
Leeches, which sickens most of their country kin.

* The Ultimate Predator — Some Garou enjoy life
in the city because it brings out their predatory instincts.
They become the ultimate hunters, choosing humans or
Leeches as their prey. To these Garou, the city is a real
urban jungle for it is far more savage, deadly and chal-
lenging than the forest where the prey are not as
intellectually challenging.

© Wealth and Power — Cities are centers of wealth
and power, and the Glass Walkers have applied their
Gifts towards gaining both. The Leeches have known
this secret for centuries. It was not just the blood which
brought them in, it was the power. Money can be a useful
weapon in the hands of those who know how to use it,
and the Glass Walkers do. City Garou also manipulate
humans, using them to increase their political influence.
Technology and allies are two other advantages which
often give city Garou the edge over their opponents.

© Weaver as Ally — Some city Garou do not see the
Weaver as an opponent. Instead, they see It as a weapon,
whether wielded by themselves or by their enemy. It is
one of the reasons for such a close connection between
the Glass Walkers and the Weaver.

Reasons for Living in the City

Many Garou have practical reasons for living in the
ible reasons players and
Storytellers may use for justifying a Garou's living in the

city. Here are a few of the pos

* Blood Bound — The Garou is a Blood Bound
servant of a vampire. Whether this is by accident or by
her own choosing, she must now serve her vampiric
master. Other Garou would look down in shame at this
character, especially if she chose this fate of her own free
will. Blood Bound Garou are usually ghouls, though not
necessarily. Ghouls tend to gain extra strength due to
the vampiric blood in their veins. They may or may not
register to Sense Wyrm, but, as Garou ghouls, they will

devise for putting Garou other than Glass Walkers and
Bone Gnawers in the city.

Glass Walkers or Bone Gnawers and most members of
these tribes are expected to live in the city.

city and the character's sense of love and devotion to

them is so strong he wants to remain with them even
after becoming Garou. Most other Garou look down
upon this excuse for remaining in the city, claiming the
Garou's old life is over and that no ties to it should
remain.

something in the city. This could be a person, a place or

political offices, high military positions and high corpo-

of property is another variation on this theme.

formation or lack of information, does not know that
most other Garou live outside the city. In fact, the

character may not even know of the existence of any
other Garou.

Typical enemies include Leeches, mages, Pentex, Weaver,

may even include other Garou.

inacab driven by a guy who seems like the typical cabby.
However, this guy is something of a shaman of the city.
He knows his way around everywhere. He can talk to the
lights. He can talk to spirits,
and his cab is a giant fetish. Best yet, he gives Garou free

streets to change the tra

tides and often shows up just when needed.

2. The Old Woman Who Always Lived There — If
the characters are trying to find out some information
and they ask a Kinfolk or one of the city Garou, they are
pointed in the direction of a little old lady who lives in
an apartment on the first floor on the corner. She has

even when they themselves were children. The little old

talk hours on end about various relatives and neighbors
and she knows whatever the characters need to know,
even if it is something she would seem to have no way of
knowing.

that many of the apartment buildings have become

seem like kindly old women who look after the people
who live in the apartments, aiding them in coming up
with the rent if possible whenever it seems they cannot
do it alone. They are often friends to the Garou who do

6. City Below the Streets — The characters stumble
upon a city located far beneath the city streets where a
community of humans live together in peace and har-
mony. They are completely untainted by the Wyrm, and
they live happy, wholesome, meaningful lives; they ap-
preciate the arts and humanities, help the homeless
above and enjoy a peace the rest of the city does not
know. They are guarded by a werecreature of some sort.

7. Human Love — A character, most likely human
born, encounters a human and falls in love. The charac-
ter has found a worthy mate for producing an offspring,
but the feelings run deeper than procreation. Will the
character leave the human and suffer great heartache? Is
there any other way?

Myth as Metaphor

There has to be a training to help you open your ears so that

you can begin to hear metaphorically instead of concretely.
— Joseph Campbell, The Power of Myth

Perhaps the most important, yet the most confusing,
setting in the Werewolf game is the Umbra, the Spirit
World that waits beyond the Gauntlet. This world does not
follow the known laws of cause and effect, or of physics —
of gravity and forces. Instead, the physics of the Umbra is
that of myth. The reality of the Umbra is metaphor.

Just what is a metaphor? Webster's New World Dictionary
defines it as follows: "a figure of speech in which one thing

it?" Haunch-Biter cried.

is spoken of as if it were another." For an example, it gives
the Shakespeare quote: "All the world's a stage." This is not
to be confused with a simile, which likens one thing to
another. A metaphor declares one thing is another.

Now, the fact-crunching rationalist in every one of us
knows this is poppycock. Of course the world is not a stage.
The rationalist can accept this premise when it is said that
the world is like a stage, for our experiences of the world do
bear a resemblance at times to activities performed on
stages during plays. But that the world is a stage is obviously
false. No, the world is a round globe orbiting a stellar body
in the void of the universe. Right?

Chapter Three: Setting

EE ES, TE

ce e/a armrranemrnansasti La

But there is another part in each and every one of us, no
matter how deeply buried, that can accept the premise that
the world is a stage: that a nonliteral, nonfactual truth can
actually be more true than what reason tells us. In some odd
way, stating that the world is a stage seems to say much
about the world: That it is more than merely a globe
orbiting a stellar body in the void of the universe. That it
has a rhyme and reason similar to a play, that most ancient
form of drama.

This is mythology. By saying the world is something our
senses tell us it clearly is not, the world is actually some-
thing more than our senses confirm. The world is not just
a set of discrete bits that happen to share a continuum;
rather the world and the bits shares qualities. It — they —
are qualities.

When a person undergoes an ordeal like that of a mythic
hero, he becomes that hero, he partakes in some mysterious
way of the myth and its magic. The movie, The Fisher King,
isan example. The experiences of Robin William's charac-
ter do not make him like the Fisher King — he is the Fisher
King. Only when Jeff Bridges's character accepts this and
treats the myth as real, as if the millionaire's cup really were
the Grail, can Robin Williams be healed.

What does all this mean in terms of running a Werewolf
game in the Umbra? It means that you, asa Storyteller, must
be aware of a way of perceiving the world that is entirely
different from the one we notice in our daily lives. As stated
above, the physics of the Umbra is that of metaphor, of
myth. The characters do not just walk down a Moon Path;
they are instead embarking on the journey to the Gates of
Hell, the Road to Paradise, the Path to Virtue, depending
on which Realm they are going to— be it Atrocity, Pangaia
or the Legendary Realm.

Each step, each action, they take in the Umbra, is
symbolic of a greater truth. The Umbra is the greater truth
of the Garou's world — the world as reality should be,
where Gaia's will is more readily discerned. But this sym-
bolism is not just confined to the spirit world; it bleeds over
into the physical world also. A Garou's actions in the
Umbra have repercussions everywhere. Slaying a Bane in
the Penumbra is not an isolated incident with no ripples of
effect. On the contrary, the meadow in the physical world
is now freed of a taint that hung over it, ataint which, while
not detectable with scientific devices or mental reasoning,
nonetheless colored the experiences of everyone who walked
there.

Umbral adventures are not merely matters of dice rolls.
The true affect of an action goes far beyond its appearances;
its symbolic affect is much more important. Theurges do
not simply recognize and identify spirits as if from a catalog
of spiritual zoology; they are trained to recognize the mean-
ing of a spirit also. While the Ahroun is concerned mainly
with whether or notshe can defeat it with a claw stroke here
or there, the Theurge must know what the meaning is, and
thus the Umbral repercussions of, defeating, binding or
even allying with a spirit.

73


Sree rare rn core ern. ve rem mar rememnerenreh

This meaning, the underlying reality of a spirit or any
being (or any object), is at the heart of what the Umbra
represents. The spirit world affects things mythically,
through their underlying meanings.

Meaningful Umbrascapes

As a Storyteller, it is important to populate the
Umbrascape with spirits which represent the meaning of a
around Wyrm sites,

place. Such meaning becomes obvious
but the meaning behind glimpsing a Naturae snake spirit
slithering through the Umbral grass may not be immedi-
Realize that in the Umbra encountering a
spirit is rarely chance — there is most always an underlying
reason. It is the job, and forte, of Theurges, and even a
knack of Galliards, to identify this meaning. It can be very

important, for

ately obvious

such occurrences often portend coming

events.

Snakes have many symbolic meanings when they appear
in dreams or myths, often very different than their "real"
world meaning of an elongated, legless reptile. They are
often guides to the Other Side, the spiritual reality of
shamans. They represent earth power or spirituality. Thus,
snake spirit, he can usually figure that
the direction it was going in is a pointer to the Other Side,
ora Realm of shamanic power. The snake symbolizes earth
magic, and thus could be a messenger from Gaia or some

when a Garou see:

Gaia totem.

In this same manner, every feature of the Umbrascape,
whether it be

fox spirit or a mere rock, should have a hint
of that symbology. In this way, every encounter is meaning-
ful, and the manner in which characters pursue or chase
these symbols can create whole stories or change the
direction of the plot. When the Theurge character chases

the snake spirit, she leads the whole pack off to the strange
hole in the ground, which ultimately leads to another
Realm or to the Wyrm Calumn they've been looking for.
On the other hand, if they decide to follow the robin
instead, it leads to the Atrocity Realm, where the pack can
stop an ongoing tragedy. (In the Middle Ages, many be-
lieved the robin tried to remove Christ's crown of thorns to
relieve his suffering. Christ's blood stained the robin's
breast red, marking the bird for its compassion ever after.)

In a sense, every story in the Umbra is a mythic cycle
story (see Chapter One); each is symbolic of deeper mean-
ings. The difference between the physical world and the
spiritual is that the meanings are easily discernible in the
spirit world. The deep layers of symbolism becomes ob-
scured by matter because the physical world is a place of
conjunction for many different symbols, while the spirit
world's symbology is often pure and undiluted.

The best resource to have here is a dictionary of ancient
symbols. This will not only provide a symbology for en-
countered spirits, but in doing so, will provide a host of
plots. The characters' understanding of the symbols inher-
ent in every Umbral being will determine how they act with
those beings.

lh —_——

The Umbra is divided into three different worlds
which follow their own paradigms. The Upper World is
a place of concepts and ideas given form and life through
spirit. It is a confusing place for the few Garou who have
entered it, as enigmatic laws prevail there. The Under-
world, or Dark Umbra, is even more confusing, for only
the dead truly know what transpires there and why. It is
the Middle World that the Garou are familiar with and
understand on an instinctual level.

The three worlds are not artificial divisions, but mytho-
logical ones. Almost all shamanic cultures the world
over recognize the three worlds, all connected and cen-
tered by the Axis Mundi, the World Tree. Shamans of
these cultures travel to the Underworld in trance to gain
healing secrets from the dead, or they entered the Upper
world, meeting strange, often angelic-seeming beings.

Garou adventures take place almost exclusively in the
Middle World, the Near Umbra described in Umbra:
The Velvet Shadow. To enter another of the worlds
requires not merely a physical or magical way of enter-
ing, but a change in consciousness. The worlds are
different reality paradigm:
requires at least a minimal understanding of them, or
being in a state conversant with that paradigm. The
most common way to enter the Dark Umbra is to die, but
that method offers no return (another method is the

ntering and perceiving them

Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

a

Silent Strider Feralia rite; see Chapter Two). Details on
these other worlds are provided in Mage sourcebooks
(the Upper, or Astral Umbra) and will be forthcoming in
Wraith sourcebooks (The Dark Umbra).

The Near Umbra of the Garou is a place of animal and
plant powers, a place where nature still holds sway, a
primitive land untouched by scientific paradigms and
rarely by the reality-warping mages. (The Dreamspeakers
usually use their magick to maintain the status quo of the
natural Umbra, unlike most other Awakened invaders to
this pristine place).

In a psychoanalytical sense, the Middle World of the
Umbra — the Garou's Near Umbra — represents the
unconscious, especially the collective unconscious. It is
all those primeval elements — the animal natures of our
psyches — still swimming around out there. It is the
ancient and now repressed spirituality of our ancestors.
It speaks to something deep within us. The Upper world
represents a more refined portion of consciousness, al-
though extremely powerful representatives from the
unconscious reside there, such as archetypal gods, while
the Underworld is the Shadow, the darkness we don't
want to recognize.

With The Ease of a Child

The Umbra is a powerful setting where many themes
and moods can be explored. But don't let it intimidate
you. All philosophy aside, the Umbra is in each and
every one of us. It is the fable and folktale, our favorite
children's story. When running stories set in the Umbra,
it is important to throw away all nervous fears of
Storytelling — am I developing the plot right? Are the
characters going to get all my carefully laid out clues?
You need to fall back into the fun of telling ghost stories
around the fire or in the darkened tent. There was no
pressure there, just the story. Remember that it is just a
story.

Try to capture some of that childhood carelessness
when taking characters to the spirit world; your games
will run much easier. The more relaxed you are, the more
open to new ideas, the more your internal muse will be
allowed to speak. Let her run with any element she
latches onto and be just as willing to drop that lead and
pick up another. Go where she guides you — remember,
this is her realm; she knows what she's doing.

And above all else, remember the quality of dreams,
for this is the stuff the spirit world is made of. As Hamlet

says: "To sleep, perchance to dream — ay, there's the
tub..." The Umbra is the rub, and a wonderful rub it is.

Developing Historical
Settings

A Storyteller may take any period in history and adapt it to
the World of Darkness. With the exception of mythical
Garou-oriented events, such as the Impergium, humans guide
history in the Gothic-Punk universe (or at least, beings who
were once human, such as vampires). Werewolves, despite
their supernatural abilities, have little power to stop the tide of
human progress. This doesn't mean that the Garou can't or
didn't make a difference at certain points in time. However,
from a historical perspective, their contributions were no
greater than that of ordinary human men and women. When
using these settings, let common sense be your guide. Charac-
ters should not tear apart George Washington before he
becomes President. For detailing historical settings, a research
trip to the library is invaluable. Finding accurate period
information, especially small details from everyday life, adds a
great deal to a story.

Tales of the Garou

These are the settings of Garou legend. Because all of these
are mythic periods, the exact times in human history are
purposely left vague. Each Storyteller should mold these times
and their mythic elements to suit her chronicle.

The First Times

A few Galliards still sing of the Forgotten Times, the First
Days. They say spirits filled the world, and there were no
totems and no tribes to divide the Garou. All werewolves
recognized each otheras brother and sister. The Wyld created,
the Weaver wove the Pattern Web and the Wyrm kept all in
balance. It was during this time that the Wyrm became
corrupt. The proud Garou broke into factions and tribes and
fought with one another. For source material on this time, see
Drums Around the Fire, Werewolf Player's Guide (the

76 Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

oa ae).

descriptions of each tribe), Umbra: The Velvet Shadow
(especially the realm of Pangaea), and the "Legends of the
Garou" in many Werewolf supplements.

During the First Days, all werewolves possessed the Back-
ground Pure Breed. If you desire, the stories could be set before
the Garou divided into tribes. The characters may witness the
division of the werewolves into separate tribes with different
Gifts. In this setting, the lupus and homid breeds will be
divided evenly. Few, ifany, metis will exist. The Gauntlet was
virtually nonexistent (only a two or three rating). Humans do
notsuffer from the Delirium. Modern Abilities are not allowed
orare replaced by ancient equivalents. For example, Firearms
becomes Archery, Drive becomes Riding, while Computer is
nonexistent. Use common sense as a guide.

In the First Days, the Garou were strong, and spirits joined
them in the defense of Gaia. This setting works well for Past
Lives. The natural beauty and spiritual glow of Gaia provides
a poignant contrast to the modern world, adding to the sense
of foreboding. A Storyteller can use memories of the First
Times to illustrate how far the Garou have fallen.

Another interesting story comes from the myths of the
Children of Gaia. Each player could create a character from a
different tribe as intertribal warfare breaks out. In the prelude,
all of the players could die as cubs, then be resurrected as the
first Children of Gaia. They could then have the unenviable

task of spreading the message of peace to the hostile tribes.
(See the Children of Gaia in the Werewolf Players Guide.)

The Great Passage

After the Wyrm spread its taintacross the lands, three tribes
of Garou were guided by Lunes to lead their Kinfolk to a Pure
Land. These were the Wendigo, the Uktena, and the Croatan.
Rage Across New York gives the details of this trek as the
Garou guided their Kinfolk across the ancient bridge, which
preceded the Bering Strait, between Siberia and Alaska. The
Garou must protect their Kinfolk from the Wyrm and survive
the Ice. The journey was a test of faith in Gaia, and many
Kinfolk and Garou failed along the way. As with any of these
chronicles, a search for research material from a local library is
invaluable for making a Storyteller's life easier. Remember
that during the time of the Passage, animals such as the woolly
mammoth still lived.

All characters must be from the Wendigo, Uktena or
Croatan tribes. If the Storyteller wants to use the Croatan
tribe, then she must create tribal Gifts for them. As in the First
Days, no modern Abilities are allowed. The Gauntlet rating is
only between three and four. The differences between the
Penumbra and the real world are almost nonexistent. Humans
do not suffer from the Delirium.

The overall theme of the Great Passage is the search for the
Pure Land. It isahegira through inhospitable wastes of ice and
tundra to escape the Wyrm. During the passage, characters
will have to protect their Kinfolk and themselves from natural
disasters, supernatural forces and the elements. Survival is one
of the most essential Skills on this journey.

|


ae AA

The Pure Land stayed free of the Wyrm's taint because of
powerful spiritual protectors. These spirits may not care if the
Garou come in. peace seeking sanctuary. Do the characters
manage to appease the spirits of the Pure Land, or do they start
awar with them? If the Garou defeat the guardians, what stops
the Wyrm's Banes from following them to the New World?

A long journey often leads to dissension and low morale.
Other Garou and wanderers may decide to turn back, only to
face the challenges of the return alone. Ifthe elders give up on
the quest, do the player characters have the strength to guide
the believers to the new land? What do the characters do if
they discover one of their Kinfolk is infected with Wyrm-
taint? Do they stop and try to cure her, abandon her to the ice,
or bring the Wyrm to the Pure Land? How do the Garou deal
with violations of the Litany? In times of trauma, people seek
comfort and companionship; metis could be born in the New
World.

The Jmpergium

A dark time came to the world when humans captured the
secret of fire and used it to subject Gaia's other children.
Humans slew fellow animals for clothing and tools, instead of
just for the meat they needed to survive. At a Garou-held
council led by the Silver Fangs, the decision was that to serve

Gaia, the Garou must keep humans in check. Quotas were set

‘on every group of humans. If the humans grew too many in
number, they were culled.

The Impergium held the human population steady for
millennia. Some Garou became especially zealous in their
killings and fell to the Wyrm. However, despite the efforts of
the Garou, people established civilization in Egypt, the Fertile

‘rescent and the East. Many werewolves blamed weak-spir-
ited members of their own society and tried to purge the
merciful. The Red Talons and Shadow Lords were the first to
find blame in others for the successes of humans.

More than failing tostop people from spreading, the Garou's
continued attacks drove the humans to powers of darkness, to
the Wyrm, to the aid of mages and the service of the undead
for protection. People hunted and killed all wild animals that
could pose any danger to them, especially the wolf. Predators
throughout the land were eliminated.

During the Impergium, no Garou may have Contacts or
Allies. There are no restrictions on the Veil. Humans don't
suffer from Delirium in the early days of the Impergium, but as
the centuries pass, more humans lose the ability to face the
fangs of the werewolves.

The War of Rage

The Garou laid the blame for the Wyrm at the feet of the
other shapeshifters. The werewolves believed that they had
done their part to stop the Wyrm. Instead of recognizing their
own failures, they blamed the Beté, Gaia's other shapeshifters
The Gurahl raised the dead, violating the sanctity of nature.

The Bastet protected the humans during the Impergium. The
Mokolé were the living shape of the Wyrm. The Ratkin
carried disease for the Wyrm. The werespiders drank blood like
the Leeches. For every other Changing Breed, the Garou
found fault. Anger boiled over in the hearts of the Ahrouns,
and the War of Rage erupted. This conflict was not an isolated
event, but rather a struggle that lasted for centuries, flaring up

in many are en after truces were reached elsewhere. In a
few places, it still goes on. The War of Rage may be set in any
time before or after the Impergium, as even the Garou aren't
sure exactly when it began. More information on the War of
Rageand other shapeshifters isgiven in the Werewolf Player'
Guide and Rage Across The Amazon.

The troupe could take the role of a pack on the front lines
of the War of Rage, perhaps in an area where the Garou are
hard pressed. Alliances of Bastet and Gurahl or Ratkin, or
Corax and Bastet using a city as a base can cause problems for
werewolves. Likewise, exotic locations, such as Africa or the
Amazon make good settings for the War of Rage. A nasty
twist to the plot might include the arrival of minions of the
Wyrm and a hesitant alliance of shapeshifters to deal with
the greater threat. The characters could even come to
admire or sympathize with their enemies, and they may
confront their elders.

Players could also be a pack sent from a foreign sept into
an area in order to play the role of "UN peacekeepers" and
stop the War. They may have to negotiate and try to ease
tensions after years of intense bloodshed. Like UN

77


Sere err rere caren aren cae ar any SP a rem umarrwrensmnarensoh

peacekeepers, they will also find themselves targets as the
conflict heats up. Can they stand by while they are attacked or
watch one side slaughtering the other?

Some players may want to take the other side in the War of
Rage. Characters could all be other shapeshifters, united in a
struggle for survival against the maddened Garou. This type of
chronicle could be a roleplaying challenge for even experi-
enced players, as each one tries to think in the mindset of a
different being.

Locations and Cultures
Ancient Egypt

With its mythology and history, Egypt provides an excel-
lent setting for Werewolf stories, especially crossovers with
Vampire: The Masquerade, Mage: The Ascension, Wraith,
and Mummy. The Silent Striders dominated Egyptamong the
Garou, although it should be possible to find Children of Gaia,
Black Furies, Bone Gnawers, Glass Walkers and even Stargaz-
ers in the mix. The Bubasti, a clan of Bastet, stayed allied with
the Silent Striders even through the War of Rage. Although
in reality the ancient Egyptians were not as obsessed with
death as many people believe, in the World of Darkness the
undead dominated ancient Egypt. The conflict between two
vampires, Set and Osiris, forced the Silent Striders from the
land. There are a myriad of sources on Egyptian history and
myths. White Wolfs Mummy supplement (for Vampire: The
Masquerade) covers most of the essentials of the myths. See
Umbra: The Velvet Shadow, for Wepauwet, a Silent Strider
who became a mummy during the Set-Osiris conflict .

Asachronicle, the war between Set and Osiris would make
a good story, because the Garou must choose between aiding
aLeech or letting an even worse servant of the Wyrm rule over
the land. Eventually, Set wins and destroys the caerns of the
Silent Striders, forcing them to flee the Nile Valley with Bane
Mummies in pursuit.

Pre-Columbian America

Before the European Invasion, the Americas were a vast
untamed wilderness inhabited by humans far more in tune
with the spiritual world than their counterparts in the Old
World. In the same way that Storytellers would adapt Greek
or Egyptian myths, Native American legends are easily ad-
justed for Werewolf: The Apocalypse. Different tribes have
their own myths and stories, so each Storyteller should make
her own decisions about which of them are true. Battles with
evil spirits or tribal warfare could be the focus of stories.

Player characters may be members of the Wendigo, Uktena
or Croatan tribes. If the Storyteller desires, perhaps a Get of
Fenris from the early Viking voyages or a Stargazer from
ancient China would be possible. If a Storyteller wants all
tribes available, then perhaps Moon Bridges to the Pure Land
could work. A few ideas for chronicles include the arrival of the
first Europeans, a Moon Bridge accident which sends foreign,
possibly Wyrm-tainted, Garou to the Pure Land and the

78
lh —_——

forming of the Gauntlet that separates the people from the
spirits. Research into Native American legends and spiritual-
ity provides good material foralmost any Werewolf chronicle.

Classical Greece

Almost everyone has heard stories from Greek mythology.
A troupe could interweave the legends of the Garou with the
myths of the Greeks. Silent Striders, Children of Gaia, Silver
Fangs, Glass Walkers and, of course, Black Furies all could be
found in ancient Greece. The Python at Delphi, the Hydra,
and numerous serpents and dragons found in the myths are
obviously servants of the Wyrm. The Labors of Hercules could
become a chronicle with afew changes. Likewise, a Storyteller
could transform the Trojan War or the Odyssey to suit her
world. The challenge of using myths as storylines is how to
make them plausible for a roleplaying troupe (while keeping
the sense of adventure). If the gods do everything (as they can
in the myths), that doesn't leave much for the characters. The
Black Furies Tribebook is a good example of how to reinter-
pret these myths from a Garou perspective. For ideas on how
to make ancient Greek stories come to life and seem realistic,
read the King Must Die or The Bull From the Sea by Mary
Renault.

Exciting scenarios can also be taken from actual Greek
history. (Every Ahroun should have fought at Thermopylae
along with the Spartans in a Past Life.) The wars of Alexander
the Great could make a good long term chronicle, involving
characters from many different tribes. Herodotus, Thucydides,
and Xenophon are historians of the time period, although
much of the modern scholarship is easier to read and explains
far more.

Medieval Europe

Shadow Lords fought with Silver Fangs for control of
Europe, while secret orders of mages scoured the land looking
forcaerns tosiphon magical energies to power their spells. The
average human lived in ignorance and fear, and the faeries still
dwelt within the deep woods. The Church held the lands
together and its power was great enough to destroy numerous
caern sites. Knights roamed the land, and castles dotted the
hills.

For the Dark and Medieval Ages, stories of Charlemagne,
Robin Hood and King Arthur, and fairy tales provide good
sources, as well as sources about more historical European
nobility. The game, Ars Magica, formerly produced by White
Wolf, contains a lot of material that easily fits into the
background of the Gothic-Punk world. Some fantasy novels
can also work as the basis for Garou in medieval Europe. The
Hunter's Hunted for Vampire: The Masquerade provides
enough details to play members of the Church devoted to
stopping the Garou. This environment is well suited for
crossovers with a historical chronicle of Mage: The Ascen-
sion.

The Inquisition can provide for a harrowing chronicle.
Werewolves could fight against the Church and its devotees
as militant orthodoxy spreads through the land, and many

Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

———

|


pagan sites (which also double as caerns) are destroyed.
Although itis not the time of the Apocalypse, the Garou must
still face an enemy that they don't have the ability to defeat.
The Black Furies call this period the Burning Times, for the
number of women who were burned as witches.

The Civil War in the United States

More Americans died during this war than any other, when
brother fought brother in a struggle for states' rights and
against slavery. Garou of the same tribes fell on both sides of
the war. Few groups remained united, excepting the Black
Furies, who helped with the Underground Railroad, the
Children of Gaia, who fought for abolition of slavery and an
end to the war, and the Wendigo and Uktena tribes, many of
whom hoped that the Europeans would destroy one another.
Many Silver Fangs, along with a few Fianna, supported the
South. The Glass Walkers stood relatively firm with the
North. It was a bloody time, and many Garou used the
opportunity to take revenge for old grievances. The Wyrm
spread throughout the land as the flames of hatred burned
high.

One interesting concept would be to have the pack's caern
located near the Mason-Dixon line. When war breaks out, the
Garou must fight to defend their home from the guerillas and
marching armies of both sides, as well as rampaging packs of
Uktena out to reclaim their land.

Another idea is to have the characters as members of the
Underground Railroad, responsible for moving slaves to the
North before the war. Then, have them decide their own roles
in the conflict. Let them see the best and worst of both the
Union and the Confederacy.

The Wyld West

Caught up in the Gold Rush, the spirit of Manifest Destiny
anda desire for land and freedoms, thousands of Americans left
the safety of the East to populate a nation that would extend
to the Pacific Ocean. As they did, they destroyed the lives of
millions of other people who already lived on the lands the
newcomers were claiming. Werewolves clashed as European
Garou traveled with their Kinfolk into the territory of the
Uktena and the Wendigo. Wyrm-consumed rail barons laid
the Weaver's metal rails across the Pure Land. Ancient
spiritual sites were disturbed by settlers. On the frontier, law
and order depended on who had the fastest gun or harshest
militia. Many good (and bad) Western movies and books can
make for fun and exciting stories and chronicles when blended
with the world of the Garou.

Werewolves could discover that settlers have built a new
town overasacred Native American site. The disturbed spirits
are angry, and the Garou must find a way to turn them from the
path of vengeance before the Wyrm corrupts them, yet, they
must make certain the spirits can survive civilization. They
may have to make a visionquest into the Umbra and endure
great tests to make peace with the spirits. In the meantime, the
spirits could compel outlaws and other undesirables to come
into the town in an attempt to drive settlers away.

ne Ae

World War JJ

The most destructive war in world history took place from
1939-1945. Europe was leveled, first by Hitler's war machine,
then by Allied invaders. Many members of the Get of Fenris
fought for Nazi Germany until they realized how evil Hitler's
dream truly was. Silver Fangs struggled to defend the Soviet
Union from the Blitzkrieg. In Africa, Silent Striders fought
against the Germans and Italians, performing commando
missions in the deserts. The Glass Walkers watched in horror
as their beautiful cities were bombed into rubble. Shadow
Lords seized power wherever they could. The Black Furies
unleashed all of their Rage against the Germans. Stargazers
harassed the Japanese during their occupation of China and
Southeast Asia. British Fianna howled in anguish for their
wounded land, while their Irish brothers and sisters were
pleased at first to see them suffer, until they too learned what
evil humans could do. Of all the Garou, perhaps the Bone
Gnawers working underground networks had the most effect
on the outcome of the war. Many werewolves believe that
World War II was the Apocalypse.

When the war finally ended, the Wyrm had spawned the
werewolves' greatest nightmare, the atomic bomb. A few
Theurges claimed that they could hear Gaia's scream echo
through the Umbra as the first mushroom cloud plumed into
the air in New Mexico.

During the war and in its aftermath, there are many
adventures for werewolves. Many caerns were obliterated or
desecrated with the blood of soldiers. In France today,
unexploded shells are still found in gardens and fields, many of
them date from World War II and some as long ago as World
War I. Underground operations, such as attempts to free
prisoners, including assaults on concentration camps, make
good stories for Garou. The Penumbra should reflect the dark
nature of the Nazi's deeds. Again, there are many sources on
World War II, but the realized horrors of Nazism may convey
an atmosphere that may be too dark even for Gothic-Punk.

Other Time Periods

Many other historical times make good material for stories
in Werewolf. The Roman Empire and its fall, the Victorian
Age, the Massachusetts Bay Colony and the Salem Witch
Trials (see Dark Colony for Vampire: The Masquerade), the
French Revolution, the Civil Rights Movement during the
sixties, the Roaring Twenties with its gangsters and prohibi-
tion, the Great Depression, the Red Scare of the fifties and
many more can all work for past lives or full-blown
chronicles. Just doa little research and let your imagination
tun free.

Chapter Three: Setting 79

Understanding DVillainy:
Splatterpunk Dillains

and? More Subtle

Antagonists
By James Moore

Edgar Allan Poe said that ‘Terror is the finest emotion,' and I'll
agree with that. Below that is the slightly grosser emotion Horror,
and below thats the Gross-out. And I go for Terror. .. butif Ican't
get Terror I'llgo for Horror, andI'mnotproud; if can' tget Horror,
I'll go for the Gross-out.

—Stephen King, Stephen King's World of Darkness Video

The World of Darkness — and most especially Werewolf:
The Apocalypse — is a world filled with blatant and often
disgusting horrors. The characters themselves frequently com-
mit gruesome murder to protect Gaia from the influence of the
Wyrm. But there is more to the villains in the game than ample
violence, or at least there should be.

Now, I think the first thing to clarify are the differences
between Terror, Horror and the Gross-out. The most com-
monly used word for excessive violence, or the Gross-out, is
Splatterpunk, a word that fits so beautifully with the word

Chapter Four: Enemies

[EE TS Te a /

Gothic-Punk that they could be close cousins — and come to
think of it, they probably are.

Splatterpunk is probably best described as excessive vio-
lence used simply to illustrate just how foul the world we live
in really is. Well, let's not kid ourselves, anyone watching the
national news can see for themselves just how twisted the
world has become. Horror, on the other hand, is most often
described by horror writers as a form of catharsis, a way of
intimately living out the fears thatassail usall; a spider running
across your bare arm, that brief second when your car hydro-
planes down the road before your car's brakes finally catch.
Lastly, that "finest of emotions," Terror. Terror is the primal
fear, the inescapable dread we all experience when the lights
go out ina storm or the air raid sirens crank into shrieking life
during the worst part of the tornado season. Terror is the
hideous sensation that someone has just walked over your
grave, the chill that ripples through your body and instinc-
tively drives you to just get the hell away from the source that
generated it. So where do the villains of Werewolf come into
this grisly little picture?

One clear look at just about any sourcebook for Werewolf
will show you more exotic enemies than most of us ever want
to see in real life. Every supernatural beastie in the Werewolf
books exposes another level of the real world, examined and
twisted to evoke outrage and fear. Read the back cover of the
rulebook, and while you're at it, take a look your closest
dictionary at the meaning of apocalypse. The entire game

81

|


Sree rare rane carne ere. ya re smreremseneren re)

is based around the fact that it's all over, these are the Final
Days. There's no more time to play Mr. Niceguy; brute
strength and violence are going to win the day, if anything
at all is going to manage that task at this time. The villains
in Werewolf reflect that mood; they, too, are desperate, and
they, too, need to have their own motivations.

Let's not make a mistake here, there is a difference
between a monster and a villain. A monster is a mindless
destructive force. Take for example a Thunderwyrm whose
only goal in life is to eat everything in sight, or take some
of the player characters I've run across. A villain has a mind
and should use that mind to his or her advantage. Some-
times monsters and villains act an awful lot alike, and that's
okay. But, as the Storyteller, you should always remember
the difference. The difference is simple motivation. Most of
the villains have goals a great deal more complex than
eating and mating. Most of the villains want something
more personal, something that the Garou are violently
opposed to. Pentex wants to corrupt the entire world. Asan
example of a villain, you could do a lot worse. Pentex is
subtle, and it is unstoppable. Pentex is very well established
in the world and operates virtually everywhere. Worst of
all, Pentex provides what are effectively necessities in the
modern world, at least from a human perspective: fuel,
cosmetics, food and entertainment. Just how long would
you go on without any or all of the above? Be truthful, now;
really think about it.

Pentex is representative of every bad aspect of the
modern world, a parody of big business. To the Garou
Pentex is a vile thing, an agent of the Wyrm; to humanity
at large, Pentex is a necessary evil and one that can be
overlooked as long as jobs and luxuries are provided by the
company. When you really think about what Pentex is all
about, you could say that Pentex and the Wyrm represent
Terror.

The Wyrm itself is inaccessible, or at least it should be.
There is no way to defeat the thing entirely, there is no way
tostop it from working its evil. There is noend todeath, and
really, that's what the Wyrm is, sentient death. Not just
physical death; we're also talking about the death of all we
believe to be "good" or "right" — the destruction of our
morals and the dissolution of our personal values. Corrup-
tion; the loss of everything good; and slow, painful loss of
innocence. Every aspect of the Wyrm is meant to terrify,
and Pentex is only one example. Wherever the Wyrm lurks,
death is sure to follow, destruction is soon to come. Worst
of all, the Wyrm is everywhere, plotting and moving,
preparing to strike. In every sense of the word, the Wyrm is
Terror.

On the next level, Horror, let's take a look at the Black
Spiral Dancers. Arguably, the Dancers should qualify in the
Gross-Out department, but I don't really agree with that.
The Dancers are a reflection, a mirror image that shows
everything foul and nasty in the typical Werewolf story.
The Dancers are vile, no two ways about it, but even the
Dancers have their own hidden agenda. Look through the

82 Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

ooo

Book of the Wyrm and read between the lines; you'll see
what I mean. They can be reasoned with, albeit not easily,
and certainly they cannot be trusted, but they are just as
able to discuss a situation as any other Garou. They are a
horrifying aspect of the game, but really no worse than the
street gangs that run rampant, killing people simply to
impress their peers or to show their superiority or worse, just
because it's fun. Their violence is mostly directed against
the Garou and for that reason, they can horrify. The Black
Spiral Dancers are the Doppleginger of the Shadow, an
example of what each of us is capable of if we surrender to
our darker impulses, the ones most of us prefer to believe
aren't there at all.

Another level of Horror can be seen in Samuel Haight,
the Garou Skinner. Haight represents madness, what any-
one can become if pushed too far. Haight's one goal is to
eradicate all of the natural Garou and to replace them with
werewolves that feel the way he does. Certainly he is
insane. Read any of the supplements where he appears and
you can see that. But even more, he represents the Unstop-
pable Menace, the serial killer. Damn it, the Garou keep
killing him, and he keeps coming back. Why? Because
Haight is the Unstoppable Menace, the unavoidable con-
frontation that the Garou would most likely rather not
approach. Haight goes beyond just being another bad guy;
if you know his history, you can see the underlying theme
that runs through all of his appearances. Ina very real
Haight represents something that most Garou do not want
to think about: the humans fighting back. Hey, let's not
ignore the obvious here, the humans suffer from both sides
of the war. The Wyrm uses them to Its best advantage, and
the Garou do not hesitate to tear the innards from anyone
they see as Wyrm-corrupt. Human beings get the shaft in
this game, and really, they don't have an easy time in any
part of the World of Darkness.

Haight is, to coin a phrase, the type of bad guy that you
love to hate. There is a certain satisfaction in stomping the
tar out of someone that has bested you countless times
before, and when they finally have succeeded, the player
characters often as not want to go back and do it all over
again.

Recurring enemies are a challenge that goes beyond dice
and rules and a vivid description. They're personal. Sports
teams always have grudge matches against the guys who
stomped them the last time they met, and player characters
need grudge matches too. There's no fun if you lose forever,
but there is a deep, powerful satisfaction in finally taking
down a personal foe. And after they finally win against a
personal foe, there are likely to be mixed emotions: regret
that the threat is finally gone forever — rather like losing
an old friend, really — and fear. Yes, fear. Is the threat
finally ended? We have the body to prove he's dead, but we
heard rumors just the other day about how he was seen over
in Topeka. How can they ever be sure? The simple answer
is that they shouldn't be sure. There should always be

ense,


co eI

doubts. When the doubts are left, the fear remains, and
sometimes even grows into Terror.

Finally, we come to the Splatterpunk villains, or as
Stephen King says, "the Gross-out." Simple examples of
Splatterpunk are the fomori and the Banes. Warped be-
yond recognition, twisted by the Wyrm's influence into
something that should not be, and basically pissed off about
the situation, the fomori make life an endless nightmare.
Name another creature that first devours a foe and then
vomits up the juiciest gobbets in a toxic stream that burns
Garou as surely as silver. The only limit to these nasty
creations are the limits that you, as the Storyteller, place on
them. They are everything leftover when you're done with
terrifying and horrifying. They are the repulsive things that
slither in the darkness, the creatures under the bed, the
escaped criminal with a hook instead of a hand. Fomori
have no redeeming characteristics, and they shouldn't.
When all else-fails to incite an emotion, you have the
fomori to fall back on.

The Banes are even worse, because the Banes can hide
and pop out into the open at the worst possible times. Banes
reflect the worst of Terror, Horror and Splatterpunk. Again
referring to the Book of the Wyrm, take a look at a few of
the creations that rest between the covers of that particular
tome and you will meet some truly repugnant monsters.
Look at the Meat Puppets. Take a few minutes to think
about what they are, and what they do, and then try to eat

a chili dog. Give some heavy thought to the Seeders, and
then go to the doctor's office for your physical. Or read over
the Dangerous Toys and then stare at one of those dolls
with the glass eyes, and see who blinks first.

Banes are a golden example not of what we fear in dark
alleys, but of what must surely be waiting just around the
next corner. They are the hidden danger that can be all too
teal. Most of us are smart enough to know better than to go
down a dark alley, but think about those who do it anyway.
A good number walk away unharmed, but a sizable amount
never walk away at all, or come away from that dark alley
scarred mentally, emotionally or both. Anyone reading this
who doesn't believe nasty things lurk in dark alleys just
hasn't been paying attention.

When you look at most of the examples I've just given,
you'll find that they've been categorized where they
shouldn't have been, at least in your eyes. Truth be told,
your eyes are just as good as mine, possibly better. I've
categorized them where I have because that's where they fit
most easily. No one category suits most of these villains,
because they have depth, dimension, a form of life all their
own. In a well told story, they are real. A sheet of statisti
doesn't make that happen. It happened because the foes
were examined in depth before they were put on paper.
Why is Pentex so nasty? Because if you take away all of the
special effects and gadgets, Pentex exists. Why is Samuel
Haight scary? Because serial killers are a fact of life. How
many serial killers are there? Too many. Even one is too
many, but there are more than one. Most of them operate
in the United States, and most of them look perfectly
innocent unless you are their victims. Worst of all, they
don't get better; they just keep getting more and more
twisted, more and more violent. Even when they are sitting
around the Christmas tree with their families, their broken
psyches are busily working on how to approach their next
victim.

Just time for one last example, the one that is scariest of
all; the player characters. The player characters normally
reflect some aspect of the players, and as such, hold certain
feelings that they share with their creators. You might find
one or two players enjoy bashing the local metis more than
the othe

These elite employees of Developmental Neogenetics Amal-

Though they disbelieve the supernatural nature of their foes, these

"evolutionarily beneficial mutations." They prepare for the field
accordingly.

for 12 shots.

stronger than steel cable). This material has an effective Strength

FBJ Special Affairs

The FBI Special Affairs division isa highly secret operation
within the Bureau, unknown to most agents and employees.
This small and woefully underfunded relic from the fifties
searches out clues and leads pointing to the actual existence of
vampires and werewolves. The members of this division are
positive about the existence of vampires — they've seen a few
of them — but they are still somewhat undecided about
werewolves. There have been many reported sightings but,
despite forensic evidence such as footprints and the like, the
division has been unable to come up with the definitive
evidence they seek. For now, they have to assume that this
werewolf business could still be a hoax.


Certain agents have come close to realizing the truth about
the Delirium in their theories and conjectures, but until they
are sure, many werewolf sightings are equated with mass
hysteria and hallucinations. The Special Affairs budget will
not allow them to follow "false" leads until they can be better
verified.

Nonetheless, the Special A ffairs is hot on the trail of some

rou, ot at least the perpetrators of the hoax. They have

discovered that werewolf sightings are sometimes linked in
place and time with missing-children reports, and by following
up on these they have come to the conclusion that the
werewolves — or hoaxers — are kidnapping children. Some-
times, these children have reappeared and never mentioned
werewolves, but it's the ones who never reappear that worry
the agents. This is beginning to appear to them as a mass
conspiracy taking place across the United State:

Special Affairs agents have Traits similar to those given for
the "Government Agents" in the Werewolf rulebook (pg.
254). The difference is that they suspect something close to
the truth, and thus are dangerous.

Chimera

Arlon Razortongue sauntered down the street — his street. Some-
body had been knocking off humans here the past few nights and Arlon
wasn't going to have any of it. He had spent years consolidating his
territory, his piece of city taf. He had thought he'd finally got the damn
Bone Gnawers to respect that, but apparently not. Arlon hoped it was

a disobedient Gnawer, rather than a vampire. His deal with the Prince
was clear, but you never know when an Anarch would ignore that

He tumed into the alley where the killings had taken place and started
searching for clues. His eyes began to glow oddly as he attuned them to
operate as spectrometers, to catch any clues invisible to normal sight. He
caughtared signal ahead of him and switched back tonormal ight. There
wasaratstandingin the middle ofthe alley staringathim, asifinchallenge
"So," Arlon thought, "it was a Gnawer." He stepped forward but
jumped back in surprise when the rat suddenly tumed into a giant, flat
grary dog-thing and charged him.

Hebarelydodgeditasitran pastandinto the street, disappearingdown
another alley. Arlon stood there sweating. That was no Garou, not the
wary it shifted, like putty porering upwards. What the hells this thing?

These bizarre creatureshave been encountered by the Garouin

the past, but very rarely, as they are innately shy and have very

effective means of camouflaging themselves. Humans have no
idea of their existence. In itsnatural form, the Chimera isa hairless
gray quadruped about the size of a cougar, but it can shift its shape

to resemble any other creature between the size of a mouse and a

large horse. It can even transform into an exact duplicate of a
human or Garou, and imitate speech, although it will usually have
only a rudimentary idea of what it is saying.

They are related to the totem spirit Chimera, but most Garou
areunaware of the exactrelationship. They are, infact, lost dreams,
dreams which have taken on a material existence of their own.
How this happens is unknown, but somehow a dream is trans-
ported by the dreamer across the Gauntlet. When this occurs, a
Chimera is sometimes created, a dream fragment become real
The Chimera has no memory of either the dream nor the
dreamer it was born from and it must forge its own identity in

a strange and alien world.

The Jersey Devil

The Jersey Devil's history is never told the same way twice: He
was the seventh son of a witch or the 13th son; he was cursed by
his mother, an old woman named Leeds, because she was sick of
bearing children; the old woman wanted achildsoshe made a pact
with old Scratch himself; the list goes on and on. The facts are all
vague, but the Jersey Devil is all too real.

Physical: Strength 9, Dexterity 5, Stamina 7

Social: Charisma 0, Manipulation 0, Appearance 0

Mental: Perception 6, Intelligence 3, Wits 4

Armor: + 3 soak

small groups. More often than not, it simply scares them for
amusement, but the rumors of young children disappearing in the
Pine Woods of New Jersey could well be attributed to the beast's
foul appetite.

Image: The Jersey Devil has shown itself in several different
variations, always with some parts that look human and features of
a large bird, a goat and a snake. The most commonly reported
features are goat hooves, a horse face, a snake tail and hawk wings.

Ecology: The Jersey Devil hasneverbeen sightedanywhere but
in the Pine Woods of New Jersey. However, the creature has been
reported repeatedly since Colonial times, normally notfarfrom the
