cr nici iT J) .

Stargazers

Obsessive Mind Games

The Stargazers are sometimes too philosophical for their
own good. If a Stargazer ever fails an Enigmas roll, he will
become obsessed with the problem and not be able to concen-
trate on anything else until he has solved it. The most elusive
mysteries gnawat us the most. During this time, the Storyteller
may rule on a host of minor effects to represent this inward
obsession (only one at a time, though). Examples include
shutting the world out (+1 difficulty to all Perception rolls) or
aslow reaction to outside stimuli (+1 difficulty to all Initiative

rolls).
Uktena

Intense Curiosity

The Uktena love secrets — except those that are kept from
them. They have an obsessive need to unravel these secrets
and must go to extremes to bring them to light (even if only for
themselves). When an Uktena learns that somebody knows
something he doesn't, he must try to pull that secret from him.
The Storyteller may impose a host of problems on the charac-
ter until he can learn the secret. Examples include trouble
concentrating (+1 difficulty for all Willpower rolls), short
temper (-1 difficulty for frenzies) or a surly disposition (-1
difficulty for Social rolls).

However, when an Uktena has gained the truth behind a
secret ina particularly ingenious or clever fashion, he may gain
a point of Wisdom Renown and a point of Willpower
(Storyteller's option).

Wendigo

Wheel of the Seasons

The Wendigo have long lived in a very close relationship
with the Earth and its ever-changing seasons. They have a
different weakness during different parts of the year, but they
gain power in winter, the season of their totem.

Spring: +1 difficulty to Willpower rolls; this represents
trouble concentrating as life quickens and grows.

Summer: -1 difficulty to frenzy rolls; summer is the tradi-
tional season of war, and this old habit is hard to break.

Fall: +1 difficulty to change forms; the world slips into
slumber and new potentials die.

Winter: -1 difficulty onsoak rolls; the Wendigoare hardiest
in this bitter season, saving up all their hate to strengthen
themselves during the harshest time of year.

Renown Conversion

The game system for keeping track of and rewarding Re-
nown changed between the original Werewolf and Second
Edition. This could cause a bit ofa problem for characters who
have been progressing on the old scale for some time. If
conversion ever gets to be too great a headache, then simply
use the old system. However, since there is a now a detailed

133


chart for Renown awards in the Second Edition, you may want
to take the time to convert.

First off, examine how much Renown the character has
now. How much did she need to get to her current rank, and
how much will she need to get to the next rank? Now look at
the new system and figure out the same thing. Youshould then
be able to make a good comparison.

Example: Ear-Biter is a Rank 3 Philodox with 13,225
Glory, 25,300 Honor and 11,500 Wisdom (his total is
50,025). He needed 40,000 Glory total to get to Rank 3,
50% of which had to be in Honor. In the new system, he
needed 3 Glory, 7 Honor and 4 Wisdom. So, we can figure
that he now has at least this amount of permanent points.

The question is, how do we calculate that extra 10,025
into temporary points?

Using the old system, he has a quarter of the Renown he
needs to get to the next rank (80,000). In the new system,
he needs 9 Honor and 7 Wisdom (he already has the 3
Glory). That is a total of 5 permanent points (or 50
temporary). A quarter of those temporary points would be
12 or 13 (let's round up— after all, he did have a little more
than a quarter).

Looking at Ear-Biter's old Renown, he has more than
twice as much Honor as Wisdom, so we can give him 9
temporary Honor and 4 temporary Wisdom. However, we
could instead give him 10 temporary Honor and 3 tempo-
rary Wisdom. This way, he is ready to petition for a
permanent point (through the Rite of Accomplishment).
This latter method will allow the gaming group to become
familiar with the new process quicker and enter some new
roleplaying opportunities with their characters' septmates.
Any method that promotes roleplaying should be the
preferred method.

So, Ear-Biter is now a Rank 3 Philodox — an Adren, as
the Garou call this rank — with 3 Glory, 7 Honor (soon to
become 8) and 4 Wisdom (with 3 temporary points).

Alternatively, some of the 13 temporary points could
have been put towards Glory, but that would not have been
to Ear-Biter's advantage yet, so let's not. He will have ample
excuses to gain Glory during play.

That's one method. If you have another, feel free to use
it. The important thing is maintain the spirit of the Re-
nown system, particularly the way Garou society rewards its
heroes and punishes the violators of sacred law.

Recommended Reading
Chapter One: Storytelling

Chronicle Creation

Aristotle's Poetics is the primer for western drama. While
it was written about Greek plays, it applies to almost all
forms of western storytelling, including written literature
and especially movies. Applying Aristotle's rules and theo-

134
i —_——

Werewolf Storytellers Handbook

ries of drama to roleplaying games maybe an odd endeavor
— Aristotle wasn't speaking of interactive drama, except in
the sense that an audience is involved in any drama — but
it would be an interesting study.

Mythic Cycle Stories

The Hero With A Thousand Faces by Joseph Campbell.
The classic book on myths, containing a more detailed
description of the mythic cycle described in the Mythic
Stories section. All of Campbell's exemplary works provide
ideas for Werewolf Storytellers, particularly The Power of
Myth (with Bill Moyers) and The Historical Atlas of World
Mythology: The Way of the Animal Powers (Volume One). All
are interesting books detailing basic information on the
importance of symbols and myths.

The Complete Grimm's Fairy Tales by Jacob and Wilhelm
Grimm. The tales provide many entertaining examples of
folk tale archetypes. Look especially for the Pantheon Press
edition with the illuminating commentary by Joseph
Campbell.

Man and His Symbols by Carl Gustav Jung. This is the
most approachable work by Jung, written for a wide audi-
ence. Jung's insights concerning myths have changed the
way we think of them, and he greatly influenced Joseph
Campbell. Any of his works can be recommended, particu-
larly The Portable Jung (edited by Campbell), Psychological
Types, The Meaning of Psychology For Modern Man, The
Psychology of the Unconscious, and Archetypes of the Collec-
tive Unconscious. All contain in-depth information on
symbolism and the human psyche.

The Women's Encyclopedia of Myths and Secrets and The
Women's Dictionary of Symbols & Sacred Objects both by
Barbara Walker. These volumes contain a wealth of basic
information on myths, symbols and their meanings.

Larousse World Mythology. This comprehensive text
covers different myths and legends from all over the world.
It comes with an index that makes locating information on
asingle figure or legend easy, even if you are uncertain of its
origins.

Chapter Three: Setting
Wilderness

Some recommended books are: Using Wild And Wayside
Plants by Nelson Coon; Indian Herbology of North America
by Alma R. Hutchens; Field & Stream magazine; Frontiers-
man magazine; National Geographic magazine; the field
manuals of the Boy Scouts and Girls Scouts of America;
Reader's Digest (you're almost guaranteed one disaster tale

per issue); and The Atlas of National Parks.

The Spirit World

An Illustrated Encyclopedia of Traditional Symbols by J. C.
Cooper. This incredible reference book gives the ancient and

a

|


ee AA

traditional symbolism behind many things, from Ablutions to
Zodiac, listing their attributions in many different cultures.
Animal-Speak: The Spiritual & Magical Powers of Creatures
Great & Small by Ted Andrews. This book includesa"compre-
hensive dictionary of animal, bird, and reptile symbolism." An

lent reference for Naturae spirit encounters.

Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy by Mircea
Eliade. Considered the classic book on shamanism, Eliade
details the cosmology of shamanic cultures, including the
three worlds (Upper, Middle, Lower) and the Axis Mundi

The Way of the Shaman by Michael Harner. A New Age
book teaching how to use shamanic techniques, it includes
basic information on shamanic cosmology. A very good
primer.

Shaman's Drum magazine. A quarterly magazine which
contains articles on all aspects of shamanism: current
indigenous peoples' methods, reprints of anthropological
studies and urban methods of shamanism. ~

Historical Settings

The People's Chronology: A Year-by-Year Record of Hu-
man Events from Prehistory to the Present by James Trager.
Although not quite up to date any longer, this book is one
of the best single sources for designing any historical
chronicle. It presents events in a yearly format and divides
them into categories, such as art, military events, etc.

The Masks of God series by Joseph Campbell. This series
ycles devel-

gives a good picture of how myths and heroic
oped in different cultures and time periods. (See "Mythic
Cycle Stories" above for more recommended Campbell
books.)

Penguin Classics. These paperback versions of the great
histories provide the reader with excellent translations of
secondary source material. A Penguin edition has been
published for almost any time period before the Industrial
Revolution.

Great Ages of Man (Time-Life Books series). This series
is well researched, colorful and easy to understand. The
books also contain maps corresponding to the age that they
cover,

A Distant Mirror, The Proud Tower and The Guns of
August by Barbara Tuchman. All very readable books on
periods in history. The first one is about the 14th century,
while thenext concerns the years 1880-1914, before WWI,
and the last is about the Great War itself.

Chapter Five: Friends

Urban Legends

The Vanishing Hitchhiker, by Jan Harold Brunvand. An
interesting study of the nature of urban legends (including
"the Hook" and "Alligators in the Sewers") and their
symbolic importance. Other books by the same author

awx——

detail even more folk legends: The Choking Doberman and

"New" Urban Legends, and The Mexican Pet: More
)" Urban Legends and Some Old Favorites

Antagonists and Friends

Folklore and mythology is always a good place to look for
new enemies to harass your Garou or allies to provide

Other

succor. In addition, there are many books on weird things.
Some recommendations are given below:

The Book of the Damned, Lo! and Wild Talents by Charles
Fort. Fort is the master of cataloguing strange and bizarre
phenomena, such as mysterious rains of frogs. He is a must
read for those interested in the weirdness hiding in the
corners and outskirts of science and reason. There is even
a journal, The Fortean Times, dedicated to weird and unex-
plained phenomena.

Other books are: The Encyclopedia of Monsters and The
Encyclopedia of the Strange by Daniel Cohen; Strange Beasts,
Giant Birds, and Bigfoot: A Bibliography of Monsters by
George M. Eberhardt; On the Track of Unknown Animals
and In the Wake of the Sea Serpents by Bernard Heuvelman;
The Humanoids by Charles Bowen; and Strange Creatures of
Time and Space by John Keel.
